The bias-absorbing trick from the worked example (a constant \(1\) feature whose weight is \(b\)) lets us write the whole dataset's predictions as one multiply:

    \[ \hat{\mathbf{y}} = \mathbf{X}\,\mathbf{w} \]

    X the design matrix, shape \(m \times (n{+}1)\) — one row per example, a leading column of ones plus one column per feature. w the weight vector of length \(n{+}1\) (its first entry is the bias). ŷ the length-\(m\) vector of all predictions at once. One matrix multiply replaces every loop. (Shaky on matrices? Revisit Module 1.)

    08 · the three-line version

    scikit-learn: same answer, less typing.

    scikit-learn wraps all of that behind fit/predict. You hand it features and targets; it finds the best \(w\) and \(b\). One catch on shape: it expects features as a 2D array (rows = examples, columns = features), so we reshape our single feature into a column. Predict what coef_ and intercept_ will be before you run it — you already know the answer from Beat 4.

    import numpy as np
from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression

x = np.array([1.0, 2.0, 3.0]).reshape(-1, 1)   # shape (3, 1): one column = one feature
y = np.array([2.0, 2.5, 3.5])

model = LinearRegression()      # by default it learns an intercept (bias) too
model.fit(x, y)                 # this is where the learning happens

print("slope  (coef_):", model.coef_)        # the learned weight(s)
print("intercept (b):", model.intercept_)    # the learned bias

# Predict for new inputs, e.g. x = 4 and x = 5
new_x = np.array([[4.0], [5.0]])
print("predictions:", model.predict(new_x))

# A built-in goodness-of-fit score (R^2): 1.0 is perfect
print("R^2 on training data:", round(model.score(x, y), 4))
    Reveal the output

    slope (coef_): [0.75] and intercept (b): 1.1666... — the same answer the normal equation gave, because under the hood it solves essentially the same least-squares problem. Predictions for \(x=4,5\) are about \(4.17\) and \(4.92\). The R^2 (about \(0.96\)) is the fraction of the target's variation the line explains — you'll meet it properly in Module 6. Three lines, a working model, and you know exactly what each one does.

    09 · the traps that bite beginners

    Five ways this lies to you.

    • "The slope proves the feature causes the target." No — regression measures association, not causation. Ice-cream sales and drowning deaths both rise in summer; a regression linking them is real, but hot weather drives both. A model can fit beautifully and still encode a confounder.
    • Forgetting residuals are squared, not absolute. One big miss hurts far more than several small ones — exactly why one outlier yanks the whole line (Beat 2).
    • Ignoring scale with multiple features. A weight of \(0.001\) on "area in m²" and \(5\) on "number of bedrooms" does not mean bedrooms matter more — the features live on different scales. Standardize before comparing importances (Module 7). The normal equation doesn't need scaling, but gradient descent in Module 4 strongly does.
    • Extrapolating far outside the data. A line fit on 10–30°C may predict negative sales at −40°C. The straight-line assumption is only trustworthy inside the range you observed.
    • Reading \( x^{(i)} \) as "x to the power i." The parenthesized superscript is an index naming the example, not an exponent. \( x^{(2)} \) is the 2nd example's feature; \( x^2 \) is x squared.
    10 · pull it from your own head

    Cold recall — answer first, then flip.

    Re-reading feels like learning and mostly isn't. Retrieving is. Answer each in your head, then tap.

    In \( \hat{y} = wx + b \), what do \(w\) and \(b\) each control?
    tap to flip
    w is the slope — tilt of the line, "drinks per extra degree." b is the intercept — height of the line, the prediction at \(x=0\).
    How do you compute MSE on three points?
    tap to flip
    For each point: prediction minus truth = residual. Square each residual. Average the squares. Square before you average — never after.
    Why does one outlier wreck a least-squares line?
    tap to flip
    Squaring makes a gap of 10 cost 100, but a gap of 1 cost only 1. The big miss dominates the total, so the line lurches to chase the outlier.
    What does the normal equation give you, and what's the catch?
    tap to flip
    \( \mathbf{w} = (\mathbf{X}^\top \mathbf{X})^{-1}\mathbf{X}^\top \mathbf{y} \) — the exact best weights in one shot, no iteration. Catch: the inverse is expensive on huge datasets, so big problems use gradient descent instead.
    Why add a column of ones to the design matrix?
    tap to flip
    It absorbs the bias \(b\) into the weight vector: the ones-column's weight is \(b\). Then \( \hat{\mathbf{y}} = \mathbf{X}\mathbf{w} \) handles slope and intercept in one multiply.
    11 · boss fight
    🤖 Boss fight — with your Claude companion

    A list of "suggested prompts" is lazy. Here's a real challenge loop. Paste it to your Claude Code companion and don't move on until you beat it cold — by hand, no calculator-from-me.

    You are drilling me on linear regression cost. Give me a tiny dataset of 3 points and a guessed (w, b). I will compute, BY HAND, every prediction, every residual, every squared residual, and the final MSE — then send you my numbers. Grade each step and show exactly where I slipped if I did. Then escalate: next round, hand me a fresh 3-point set and make me solve the normal equation by hand for the best w and b, and verify my MSE drops below the guess. Mix in one round where you move a single point to an extreme and ask me to predict which way the best line tips BEFORE I compute. Keep going until I get two full rounds perfect in a row.

    You pass when you can score any line in your head, solve a 2×2 normal equation without notes, and explain — using the outlier picture, not a memorised rule — why squaring the residuals is the whole point.

    12 · sharpen it

    A few to grind (solutions hidden).

    1. Using \( \hat{y} = 2x + 1 \), compute predictions for \( x = 0, 3, 10 \) and state the slope and intercept in plain words.
      Show solution

      \( \hat{y}(0)=1 \), \( \hat{y}(3)=7 \), \( \hat{y}(10)=21 \). Slope 2: the prediction rises by 2 for every +1 in \(x\). Intercept 1: the prediction is 1 when \(x=0\).

    2. For \( (1,1),(2,3),(3,4) \) with guessed \( w=1, b=0 \), compute each residual, each square, and the MSE.
      Show solution

      Predictions \( \hat{y}=x \): \(1,2,3\). Residuals: \(0, -1, -1\). Squares: \(0,1,1\). MSE \(=(0+1+1)/3=2/3 \approx 0.667\).

    3. You fit \( \hat{y} = 0.8\,x_1 + 25\,x_2 + 40 \) for an electricity bill, with \(x_1\) = floor area (m²) and \(x_2\) = number of AC units. Interpret each weight and the intercept.
      Show solution

      The \(0.8\) on \(x_1\): each extra m² adds about \$0.80 to the predicted bill, holding AC count fixed. The \(25\) on \(x_2\): each extra AC adds about \$25, holding area fixed. The intercept \(40\): a (hypothetical) home with zero area and zero AC is predicted \$40 — usually not physically meaningful, just the baseline. "Holding the other fixed" matters: each weight is its feature's effect with the others held constant.

    4. Set up the normal equation for \( (0,1),(1,3),(2,5) \) and find \(w\) and \(b\). Verify by predicting at the three points.
      Show solution

      \( \mathbf{X}=\begin{bmatrix}1&0\\1&1\\1&2\end{bmatrix} \), \( \mathbf{y}=\begin{bmatrix}1\\3\\5\end{bmatrix} \). Then \( \mathbf{X}^{\top}\mathbf{X}=\begin{bmatrix}3&3\\3&5\end{bmatrix} \), \( \mathbf{X}^{\top}\mathbf{y}=\begin{bmatrix}9\\13\end{bmatrix} \). Determinant \(=3(5)-3(3)=6\), inverse \(=\tfrac16\begin{bmatrix}5&-3\\-3&3\end{bmatrix}\). Weights \(=\tfrac16\begin{bmatrix}5(9)-3(13)\\-3(9)+3(13)\end{bmatrix}=\tfrac16\begin{bmatrix}6\\12\end{bmatrix}=\begin{bmatrix}1\\2\end{bmatrix}\). So \(b=1, w=2\), i.e. \( \hat{y}=2x+1 \). Check: \(x=0\to1\), \(x=1\to3\), \(x=2\to5\) — exact fit (MSE \(=0\)), since the points are perfectly collinear.

    5. A friend regresses temperature on ice-cream sales, then sales on temperature, and is shocked the two slopes aren't reciprocals. What do you tell them?
      Show solution

      Neither is wrong — they minimise different residuals. Regressing \(y\) on \(x\) shrinks vertical gaps in \(y\); the reverse shrinks horizontal gaps in \(x\). Only with a perfect (\(R^2=1\)) fit do the slopes become exact reciprocals; with any scatter they differ. The lesson echoes the causation trap: regression is directional and descriptive, not a symmetric physical law. Pick the direction that matches what you actually want to predict.

    Lock this in. The cost \( J(w,b) = \frac{1}{m}\sum(\hat{y}-y)^2 \) you just built by hand is exactly the hill that Module 4 — Gradient Descent rolls downhill to find the best \(w\) and \(b\). The normal equation jumps to the bottom in one leap; gradient descent walks there step by step — and is the only option once the dataset is too big to invert. Same cost, same minimum, two ways down the same hill. And this whole \( \hat{\mathbf{y}} = \mathbf{X}\mathbf{w} \) primitive returns as the literal last layer of the neural networks in Module 10.

    You beat this module when you can score any line in your head, solve a 2×2 normal equation cold, and explain why squaring the residuals is the entire game — not when you've merely read it.

    Module 4

    Gradient Descent: How Models Learn

    In Module 3 we said "find the \( w \) and \( b \) that make the cost small" — and then quietly skipped the only interesting part: how. This is the how. It's one line of math, run a few billion times, and it trains everything from a straight line to ChatGPT. You're not going to read about it. You're going to be it, break it, debug it, and get quizzed until it's boring.

    01 · the problem, before the lesson

    You're a robot, lost in fog

    You wake up somewhere on a hillside. The fog is so thick you can't see your own hands, let alone the valley below. You have to get to the lowest point. You have exactly one sense: you can feel the slope of the ground under your feet — which way it tilts, and how steeply.

    That's the whole game. No map. No view of the bottom. Just the tilt beneath you, here, now. What's your move? Don't scroll — actually decide your strategy in one sentence before you read on.

    Got it? Here's the thing: you almost certainly just invented gradient descent. The instinct is "feel which way the ground falls, step that way, repeat." That instinct, written down carefully, trains every model in this course. The rest of this module is making that instinct precise enough to give to a computer — and finding the two stupid-simple ways it blows up.

    One trap, flagged early. If you only ever walk downhill, you can get stuck in a shallow dip that isn't the deepest valley — a local minimum. For the bowl-shaped costs in this module there's only one valley, so it never bites. It comes back to haunt us in Module 10 (neural nets), where the landscape is full of dips.

    02 · lock in a guess

    Predict before you peek

    You figured out the rule: step in the direction the ground goes down — opposite the slope. Steeper ground, bigger step you can afford. Flat ground, you're basically there. Fine. Now commit to this before we go further, because the answer is the single most common way training explodes.

    You make each step proportional to the slope (steep = big step). On a very steep wall, you get greedy and crank that step size way up. What happens?

    A big step on a steep slope flings you past the bottom and up the opposite wall — where it's also steep, so the next big step flings you back, even further. The size of the step has a name: the learning rate. Getting it wrong is most beginners' first real bug, and you'll watch it happen live in the lab two beats from now.

    Opposite problem. You're terrified of overshooting, so you make every step tiny — like 0.001 of the slope. Direction is always correct. What's the catch?

    Tiny steps are correct and useless. Move 0.006 toward a target three units away and do the math on how long that takes. The learning rate has a Goldilocks zone: too big diverges, too small crawls, and the sweet spot is narrower than beginners expect. Finding it is a real engineering decision, not a footnote.

    03 · now (and only now) the math

    You already invented it — here's the notation

    The "height of the hill" is the cost \( J(w) \): one number that says how wrong the model is, as a function of a knob \( w \) we can turn. The "slope you feel" is the derivative \( \frac{dJ}{dw} \): how fast the cost changes when you nudge \( w \). Read that slope as the answer to one question:

    "If I nudge \( w \) up a hair, does the cost go up or down, and how fast?" Positive slope → cost rises as \( w \) grows → you should decrease \( w \). Negative slope → you should increase \( w \). Near zero → flat ground, probably the bottom.

    Your whole foggy-robot strategy is exactly one line:

    \[ w \;\leftarrow\; w \;-\; \alpha\,\frac{dJ}{dw} \]

    w the knob you're tuning. dJ/dw the slope — which way is uphill and how steep. α (alpha) the learning rate, your step size, a small positive number you choose. − the magic minus sign: subtracting the slope walks you downhill.

    Read it out loud: "new knob = old knob, minus a little bit of the slope." That little bit is \( \alpha \). The minus sign is the entire trick — if the slope is positive (cost climbing as \( w \) grows) you subtract a positive number and \( w \) shrinks; if the slope is negative you subtract a negative and \( w \) grows. Either way, you move toward lower cost. \( \alpha \) is a hyperparameter: a dial you set by hand, not something the algorithm learns.

    The whole of training — linear regression, logistic regression, the network behind ChatGPT — is this line, run millions of times. That is not a metaphor. It is literally this line.

    When there are two knobs (\( w \) and \( b \)), apply the same rule to each, using each one's own slope (now called a partial derivative, \( \frac{\partial J}{\partial w} \) — "partial" just means "slope in one knob's direction, holding the other fixed"):

    \[ w \leftarrow w - \alpha\,\frac{\partial J}{\partial w}, \qquad b \leftarrow b - \alpha\,\frac{\partial J}{\partial b} \]

    Compute both slopes first from the current \( (w,b) \), then update both. Never update \( w \) and recompute \( b \)'s slope from the changed \( w \) — they take one synchronized step together. The full list of partials is called the gradient, written \( \nabla J \). It points the fastest way uphill; we go the opposite way. That's the entire algorithm.

    04 · be the algorithm (by hand)

    Run it in your own head first

    Forget data for a second. Take the cleanest possible hill: \( J(w) = (w-3)^2 \). It's a bowl with its lowest point obviously at \( w = 3 \) (cost 0 there). Pretend you don't know that and let gradient descent find it. The slope is \( \frac{dJ}{dw} = 2(w-3) \) (chain rule: power comes down, times the derivative of the inside, which is 1). Start at \( w = 0 \), learning rate \( \alpha = 0.1 \), so the rule is \( w \leftarrow w - 0.1\cdot 2(w-3) \).

    Do Step 1 yourself before you open the answer. At \( w = 0 \): what's the slope, and where does the step land you?

    Check Step 1

    Slope \( = 2(0-3) = -6 \). Update: \( w = 0 - 0.1\times(-6) = 0 + 0.6 = \mathbf{0.6} \). The slope was negative (cost falls as \( w \) rises), so you moved right, toward 3. Correct direction.

    Now the rest of the march — verify each line:

    • Step 1. \( w=0 \): slope \( 2(0-3)=-6 \). \( w = 0 - 0.1(-6) = \mathbf{0.6} \).
    • Step 2. \( w=0.6 \): slope \( 2(0.6-3)=-4.8 \). \( w = 0.6 - 0.1(-4.8) = \mathbf{1.08} \).
    • Step 3. \( w=1.08 \): slope \( 2(1.08-3)=-3.84 \). \( w = 1.08 + 0.384 = \mathbf{1.464} \).
    • Step 4. \( w=1.464 \): slope \( 2(1.464-3)=-3.072 \). \( w = 1.464 + 0.3072 = \mathbf{1.7712} \).

    The values march \( 0 \to 0.6 \to 1.08 \to 1.464 \to 1.7712 \to \dots \), each step smaller than the last — because the slope keeps shrinking as you near the bottom. Gradient descent is self-braking: steep ground = big steps, flat ground = tiny steps, so it glides into the minimum instead of blowing past it (as long as \( \alpha \) isn't too big — hold that thought).

    There's a tidy pattern hiding here. Each step, the distance to the target multiplies by \( (1 - 2\alpha) = 0.8 \). Check it: distance starts at 3; after step 1 it's \( 3 - 0.6 = 2.4 = 0.8\times 3 \); after step 2 it's \( 3 - 1.08 = 1.92 = 0.8\times 2.4 \). So \( w \) never quite reaches 3 — it gets exponentially close. That's why we run "enough" iterations instead of expecting a perfect landing. Remember this multiplier; it's about to tell us exactly when things explode.

    05 · the lab — three missions

    Automate it, then break it on purpose

    Now the computer applies \( w \leftarrow w - \alpha\cdot 2(w-3) \) for you, one step per click (or hit Run). This is the same bowl you just walked by hand. Your job isn't to "play with it" — it's to complete three missions. Drag the learning-rate slider and watch the slope line flatten as the ball nears the bottom.

    LAB gradient descent, with stakes

    Start at \( w = 0 \). The ball sits at the current \( w \); each Step does \( w \leftarrow w - \alpha\cdot 2(w-3) \). Watch the cost readout — is it shrinking or growing?

    • Reach the bottom in 8 steps or fewer Get the cost under 0.05 (w ≈ 3). Small α crawls — go bigger, but not too big. Try around α = 0.3.
    • Make it diverge on purpose Crank α until the cost shoots UP instead of down. This is the overshoot you predicted in Beat 02.
    • Find the edge of stability Get it to bounce side to side without diverging — a steady oscillation that never settles and never explodes. It lives right around α = 1.0.

    When you nailed Mission 3 you found something deep: there's a razor-sharp threshold where the same algorithm flips from "converges" to "explodes." That threshold is what makes choosing \( \alpha \) a real decision.

    Before you compute it: with the distance-multiplier rule \( (1 - 2\alpha) \), what's the exact \( \alpha \) where the bowl tips from converging to diverging?

    The multiplier is \( (1-2\alpha) \). Convergence needs its magnitude below 1. At \( \alpha = 1.0 \) it's exactly \( -1 \): from \( w=0 \) you bounce \( 0 \to 6 \to 0 \to 6 \to\dots \), mirror-imaged across 3, never closer, never farther. Above 1.0 the magnitude exceeds 1 and the distance grows every step — divergence. That's the edge you felt in Mission 3.

    06 · fix the broken loop

    This code looks right. It isn't.

    A beginner wrote this to descend our bowl. It runs without errors — and the cost grows every single step instead of shrinking. Read it. Find the bug before you open the answer. There is exactly one wrong character.

    def train(w=0.0, lr=0.1, steps=20):
    for i in range(steps):
        grad = 2 * (w - 3)        # slope of J(w) = (w-3)**2
        w = w + lr * grad         # take a step   # <-- something is wrong on this line
        print(i, round(w, 3), round((w - 3) ** 2, 3))
    return w
    Show the bug & why

    w = w + lr * grad walks uphill. The gradient points toward higher cost, so to go down you must subtract it: w = w - lr * grad. That one flipped sign is the difference between a model that learns and a model that screams off to infinity. Note this is a different failure from the lab: there, divergence came from too-big a step in the right direction; here, every step is the right size but the wrong way. Two ways to break the same one-line rule.

    07 · from one knob to real data

    The same line, now with x's and y's

    The bowl was a warm-up. Real linear regression has two knobs and actual data. The prediction for example \( i \) is \( \hat{y}_i = w x_i + b \), and with \( n \) examples the mean squared error cost is:

    \[ J(w,b) = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}\left(\hat{y}_i - y_i\right)^2 = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}\left(w x_i + b - y_i\right)^2 \]

    x_i the input for example \( i \). ŷ_i the model's guess. y_i the truth. ŷ_i − y_i the residual (how far off you are). Squaring makes every error positive and punishes big misses harder. Σ "add over all n examples."

    Differentiate that (chain rule; the factor of 2 falls out of the square) and you get the two slopes you'll actually code:

    \[ \frac{\partial J}{\partial w} = \frac{2}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}\left(\hat{y}_i - y_i\right)x_i, \qquad \frac{\partial J}{\partial b} = \frac{2}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}\left(\hat{y}_i - y_i\right) \]

    Read them in words. The \( b \)-slope is twice the average residual: predicting too low everywhere → residuals negative → slope negative → update raises \( b \). The \( w \)-slope is the average residual weighted by \( x_i \): an example with a big \( x \) pulls harder, because changing \( w \) moves its prediction more.

    Now do one real step by hand. Two points: \( (x_1,y_1)=(1,2) \) and \( (x_2,y_2)=(2,3) \). Start at \( w=0, b=0 \), \( \alpha=0.1 \), \( n=2 \). Try to get all the way to the updated \( w \) and \( b \) before opening the answer.

    Check your one full GD step

    Predict. \( \hat{y}_1 = 0\cdot 1 + 0 = 0 \), \( \hat{y}_2 = 0\cdot 2 + 0 = 0 \).

    Residuals \( (\hat{y}_i - y_i) \): \( 0-2 = -2 \) and \( 0-3 = -3 \). Both negative — predicting too low, as expected from an all-zero model.

    Slope for w: \( \frac{2}{2}\big[(-2)(1) + (-3)(2)\big] = 1\times(-2-6) = -8 \).

    Slope for b: \( \frac{2}{2}\big[(-2)+(-3)\big] = 1\times(-5) = -5 \).

    Update both at once. \( w = 0 - 0.1\times(-8) = \mathbf{0.8} \); \( b = 0 - 0.1\times(-5) = \mathbf{0.5} \).

    Did cost actually drop? Before: \( J = \frac{1}{2}\big[(-2)^2+(-3)^2\big] = \frac{1}{2}(4+9) = 6.5 \). After: \( \hat{y}_1 = 0.8(1)+0.5 = 1.3 \), \( \hat{y}_2 = 0.8(2)+0.5 = 2.1 \); residuals \( (-0.7,-0.9) \); \( J = \frac{1}{2}(0.49+0.81) = 0.65 \). Cost fell from 6.5 to 0.65 in a single step. That's gradient descent earning its keep.

    08 · how much data per step?

    Batch, mini-batch, stochastic

    Every gradient formula above sums over all \( n \) examples. With a few hundred rows, fine. With millions — or the trillions of tokens an LLM trains on — computing the full sum before every step is absurdly slow. The three flavours differ in exactly one thing: how much data you look at before taking each step.

    • Batch GD. Use all examples per step. Gradient is exact, path is smooth and stable, each step is expensive. This is what we'll code from scratch — it's the clearest.
    • Stochastic GD (SGD). Use one random example per step. Dirt cheap, so you take many steps fast, and the noise can even jiggle you out of shallow dips. But each gradient is a rough estimate, so the path zig-zags.
    • Mini-batch GD. Use a small group — typically 32 to 256 examples — per step. The sweet spot everyone actually uses: far cheaper than batch, far less noisy than pure SGD, and it maps beautifully onto GPUs. One full pass over all the mini-batches is an epoch.

    Mental model: batch GD takes a few confident strides; SGD takes many tipsy little steps; mini-batch takes brisk, mostly-sober steps. All three head downhill — they just trade accuracy-per-step against steps-per-second. You'll lean on mini-batch SGD constantly once we hit neural nets in Module 10 and Module 11.

    09 · from scratch, then the library

    Predict the output, then run it

    Here's the whole loop in NumPy — the exact MSE gradients you just derived, fitting a line to data that secretly follows \( y \approx 2x + 1 \) plus noise. Before you run it, predict: after 1000 epochs, will \( w \) land closer to 2 or to 0? Will the cost keep dropping forever, or hit a floor?

    import numpy as np

# --- make some data that roughly follows y = 2x + 1 ---
rng = np.random.default_rng(0)          # fixed seed -> reproducible
x = np.linspace(0, 5, 50)               # 50 inputs from 0 to 5
y = 2.0 * x + 1.0 + rng.normal(0, 0.5, size=x.shape)   # true line + noise
n = len(x)

# --- start the knobs at zero ---
w, b = 0.0, 0.0
alpha = 0.01                            # learning rate
epochs = 1000                           # how many full passes (batch GD)

def cost(w, b):
    yhat = w * x + b
    return np.mean((yhat - y) ** 2)     # mean squared error

for epoch in range(epochs):
    yhat = w * x + b                    # predictions for ALL points
    error = yhat - y                    # residuals (y-hat minus y)
    # exact MSE gradients:
    dw = (2 / n) * np.sum(error * x)    # dJ/dw
    db = (2 / n) * np.sum(error)        # dJ/db
    # one synchronized step downhill:
    w = w - alpha * dw
    b = b - alpha * db
    if epoch % 200 == 0:
        print(f"epoch {epoch:4d}  cost {cost(w, b):.4f}  w {w:.3f}  b {b:.3f}")

print(f"\nlearned:  w = {w:.3f},  b = {b:.3f}   (true was w=2, b=1)")

    Now run it. The cost drops every line and the parameters climb toward the truth. Roughly:

    epoch    0  cost 31.1602  w 0.395  b 0.121
epoch  200  cost 0.1789   w 2.130  b 0.729
epoch  400  cost 0.1784   w 2.121  b 0.757
epoch  600  cost 0.1784   w 2.118  b 0.768
epoch  800  cost 0.1783   w 2.117  b 0.772

learned:  w = 2.116,  b = 0.773   (true was w=2, b=1)

    Read the story in those numbers. The cost crashes from over 30 down to about 0.18 and then stops. That floor isn't failure — it's the leftover noise we sprinkled in (you can't fit randomness). With noise standard deviation 0.5 you'd expect a floor near \( 0.5^2 = 0.25 \); we land a touch below it (about 0.18) because the fitted line soaks up a little of the random wiggle in these particular 50 points. Learned \( w \approx 2.12 \) sits right next to the true 2; \( b \approx 0.77 \) lands near the true 1, missing slightly because noise in a small sample tugs the best-fit line off the underlying truth. Two things you'd flag as broken: a cost that rises (α too high) or one still falling fast at the last epoch (run more epochs, or raise α). A different seed gives slightly different numbers — expected and fine.

    Once you've felt the loop, scikit-learn does it for you. SGDRegressor is linear regression trained by (mini-)stochastic gradient descent — the library version of everything above. It forces one real-world habit on you: scale your features first (a skinny, lopsided cost bowl makes GD zig-zag painfully). We chain a scaler and the regressor in a Pipeline. Predict the printed prediction at x = 2.5 before running.

    import numpy as np
from sklearn.linear_model import SGDRegressor
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
from sklearn.pipeline import make_pipeline

rng = np.random.default_rng(0)
x = np.linspace(0, 5, 50)
y = 2.0 * x + 1.0 + rng.normal(0, 0.5, size=x.shape)
X = x.reshape(-1, 1)                    # sklearn wants a 2D array of features

model = make_pipeline(
    StandardScaler(),                  # center & scale x  -> faster, stabler GD
    SGDRegressor(loss="squared_error", # plain MSE (the cost we used)
                 learning_rate="constant", eta0=0.05,  # eta0 is alpha
                 max_iter=1000, random_state=0)
)
model.fit(X, y)

pred = model.predict([[2.5]])          # predict y when x = 2.5  (truth ~ 6.0)
print(f"prediction at x=2.5: {pred[0]:.3f}")
print(f"R^2 on training data: {model.score(X, y):.3f}")

    It prints a prediction near \( 6.0 \) for \( x = 2.5 \) (since \( 2\times 2.5 + 1 = 6 \)) and an \( R^2 \) close to \( 1.0 \) — the line explains almost all the variation in \( y \). Note eta0 is scikit-learn's name for \( \alpha \). For a closed-form, no-learning-rate fit you'd reach for LinearRegression (Module 3); SGDRegressor is the one that scales to huge data and shares its DNA with neural-network training.

    10 · the traps that actually get people

    Where beginners lose hours

    • "Bigger learning rate = faster learning." Up to a point — then it flips. Too big and the cost grows every step until it overflows to nan. If cost ever rises, first fix is shrink \( \alpha \) by 10x.
    • Forgetting to scale features. One feature ranges 0–1, another 0–100000 → the cost bowl becomes a long skinny ravine, and GD zig-zags across the narrow direction forever. Standardize (subtract mean, divide by std). Deep dive in Module 7.
    • Updating one parameter with the other's already-updated value. Compute all gradients from the current parameters first, then apply every update. Interleaving breaks the math.
    • Thinking GD always finds the best possible answer. For convex (bowl-shaped) costs like linear and logistic regression, it does. For neural nets the landscape has many valleys; GD finds a good minimum, not provably the global one. Good-enough is what we ship.
    • Confusing learning rate with epochs. \( \alpha \) sets how far each step goes; epochs set how many. A too-small \( \alpha \) can be partly rescued by more epochs; a too-big \( \alpha \) cannot be fixed by any number of epochs — it diverges.
    • Dropping the factor of 2 in the gradient. Using \( \frac{1}{n} \) instead of \( \frac{2}{n} \) nothing breaks catastrophically — it's just like halving your learning rate. But know which convention you're on so \( \alpha \) means what you think.
    11 · cold recall — no scrolling back

    Pull it out of your own head

    Re-reading feels like learning but mostly isn't. Retrieving is. Answer each in your head first, then tap to flip. If you can't, that's the signal to scroll up — but try first.

    In \( w \leftarrow w - \alpha\frac{dJ}{dw} \), what is \( \alpha \) and what does it control?
    tap to flip
    \( \alpha \) is the learning rate — the size of each step. Too small: painfully slow. Too big: overshoot and diverge.
    Why the minus sign?
    tap to flip
    The gradient points uphill (toward higher cost). Subtracting it moves you downhill, toward the minimum.
    Your cost stops dropping and starts rising fast. First thing to check?
    tap to flip
    Learning rate too high (overshooting) — or a sign error walking uphill. Lower \( \alpha \) by 10x first.
    Batch vs SGD vs mini-batch — the one-line difference?
    tap to flip
    How much data per step: all (batch, exact & slow), one (SGD, cheap & noisy), 32–256 (mini-batch, the practical sweet spot).
    One sentence: what IS gradient descent?
    tap to flip
    Repeatedly nudge the knobs a little bit opposite the slope of the error, until you reach the bottom.
    12 · boss fight

    Beat your Claude companion

    🤖 Boss fight — with your Claude companion

    A box of "suggested prompts" is lazy. Here's a real challenge loop. Paste it to your Claude Code companion and don't move on until you beat it cold — three steps by hand, no calculator-from-Claude.

    You are quizzing me on gradient descent. Give me a cost function J(w) and its derivative, a starting w, and a learning rate. I will do 3 update steps BY HAND and tell you my final w and cost. Then grade each step, show exactly where I went wrong if I did, and hit me with a harder one — including at least one case where the learning rate makes it diverge, and ask me to PREDICT converge-or-diverge before I compute. Keep escalating until I get two in a row perfect, then ask me to explain, in plain words using the robot-in-fog picture, WHY a too-big alpha diverges.

    You pass this module when you can run three steps in your head, predict converge-vs-diverge from \( \alpha \) alone, and explain the blowup with the fog picture — not a formula you memorized.

    Stretch: take it further

    Reading about descent isn't feeling it. Pick one and code it end to end.

    1. Learning-rate sweep. Wrap the from-scratch loop in a function that returns the cost history. Run it for \( \alpha \in \{0.001, 0.01, 0.1, 0.3, 0.6\} \), plot all curves on one chart with a log-scaled y-axis, and write one sentence per curve. Success: you can point to the smallest \( \alpha \) that still converges in 1000 epochs and the one where it starts to diverge.
    2. From-scratch fitter on real data. Reuse the NumPy loop on scikit-learn's diabetes dataset using one feature (BMI, column index 2). Standardize \( x \), run GD, plot the fitted line, and beat a "predict the mean" baseline on MSE. Success: your MSE is clearly below the baseline and the cost curve flattens instead of rising.
    3. Batch vs mini-batch vs SGD. Generate 2000 noisy points from \( y \approx 2x+1 \). Implement all three (mini-batch size 32, shuffled each epoch) and plot cost vs. examples seen. Success: batch is smoothest, SGD noisiest, mini-batch between — all reaching a similar final cost.
    Lock this in. You will see \( w \leftarrow w - \alpha\cdot\text{gradient} \) again, unchanged: in Module 5 (logistic regression reuses it for free), in Module 10 (where "backpropagation" is just a clever way to compute the gradient this loop uses — that's the only difference between them, and mixing the two up is the #1 beginner error), and in Module 11 (Adam is this same idea with a smarter, self-adjusting step size). One line. The whole field.
    Module 5

    Logistic Regression & Classification

    01 · the moment linear regression breaks

    An email lands. Spam, or not?

    You spent Module 3 building a model that predicts a number: \( z = wx + b \), a straight line. It is good at "how much will this house sell for." Now I hand you a new job: is this email spam — yes or no?

    So you do the obvious thing. You label spam as 1 and not-spam as 0, you fit a line through it, and you say "if the line is above 0.5, call it spam." Reasonable. Watch it fail.

    Here is the count of suspicious links in an email (\( x \)) versus its true label (\( y \), 0 or 1). Fit a straight line through those dots in your head. Now answer:

    You fit a line to 0/1 labels. One email shows up with 40 suspicious links — wildly spammy. What number does your straight line predict for it?

    A straight line does not know that probabilities live between 0 and 1. Feed it a big \( x \) and it cheerfully returns 4.7, or −2.3 for a clean email. "This email is 470% spam" is gibberish. Worse: one extreme outlier tilts the whole line, dragging the 0.5 crossing point and misclassifying emails that were fine before. Linear regression is the wrong shape for yes/no. We need an output that can't escape the 0-to-1 box.

    02 · the fix is a dimmer switch

    Don't kill the line. Squash it.

    The line itself is fine as a measure of evidence. Big positive \( z = wx+b \) means "tons of evidence for spam." Big negative means "tons of evidence for clean." Near zero means "on the fence." Keep that. The problem is only the final number escaping the box.

    So bolt on a second step: take the unbounded evidence \( z \) and run it through an S-shaped squashing function that physically cannot leave 0 and 1. Think of a dimmer switch, not a light switch. A light switch is brutal — fully on or fully off. The dimmer slides smoothly: far-left is off (probability 0), the middle is half-bright (0.5), far-right is full (probability 1). Crank the dial to the extreme and the light barely changes; near the middle a tiny turn swings it a lot.

    That dimmer is the sigmoid. Before I show you the formula, predict its behaviour:

    The sigmoid takes any \( z \) from −∞ to +∞ and returns a probability. What does it return when the evidence is exactly zero, \( z = 0 \)?

    Zero evidence is the definition of "I have no idea" — a coin flip, 0.5. That is the pivot point of the whole curve. Push \( z \) positive and it slides up toward 1; push it negative and it slides down toward 0, but it never reaches either. There is always a sliver of doubt. That smoothness is not decoration — it is exactly what lets gradient descent feel which way to nudge the dial.

    03 · now the notation you earned

    You just invented the sigmoid.

    Two steps, written down. First the same old linear score from Module 3. Then squash it.

    \[ z = wx + b \qquad\qquad \sigma(z) = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-z}} \]

    x the input feature (e.g. link-count). w the weight — how strongly \( x \) pushes toward "yes." b the bias — slides the curve left/right. z the raw evidence, any real number. σ (sigma) the sigmoid, the dimmer switch. e ≈ 2.718, Euler's number, a fixed constant.

    Read the formula like a sentence. When \( z \) is large positive, \( -z \) is large negative, so \( e^{-z} \) is tiny, the denominator is ≈ 1, and \( \sigma(z) \approx 1 \). When \( z \) is large negative, \( e^{-z} \) is huge, the fraction is tiny, and \( \sigma(z) \approx 0 \). When \( z = 0 \), \( e^{0}=1 \), so \( \sigma(0) = \frac{1}{1+1} = 0.5 \) — the coin flip you predicted. One formula, the whole S.

    The model's predicted probability that the example is class 1 is:

    \[ \hat{y} = \sigma(wx + b) \]

    ŷ ("y-hat") our estimate of \( P(\text{class}=1) \). Predicted class is 1 if \( \hat{y} \ge 0.5 \), else 0. Since \( \sigma(z) \ge 0.5 \) exactly when \( z \ge 0 \), the decision boundary sits wherever \( wx + b = 0 \).

    That boundary is the dividing line between "predict yes" and "predict no." With one feature it's a single point on the number line; with two features it's a straight line on a plane. And the 0.5 threshold? Just a default. You can move it — more on that when it bites you in Module 6.

    Free reuse. Notice nothing here is new except the squash. The z = wx + b is Module 3, unchanged. In Beat 07 the optimiser will be Module 4's gradient descent, unchanged. You're adding one twist, not starting over.
    04 · do it by hand before the machine does

    Squash three scores yourself.

    Grab \( e \approx 2.718 \). Cover the answers and compute \( \sigma(z) \) for \( z = 0 \), \( z = 2 \), and \( z = -1 \) before you peek. You only understand the dimmer once your own hand has turned it.

    Check my sigmoid arithmetic

    \( z = 0 \): \( e^{-0} = 1 \), so \( \sigma(0) = \frac{1}{1+1} = 0.5 \). Zero evidence, a perfect coin flip.

    \( z = 2 \): \( e^{-2} = \frac{1}{2.718^2} = \frac{1}{7.389} \approx 0.135 \). Then \( \sigma(2) = \frac{1}{1 + 0.135} = \frac{1}{1.135} \approx 0.881 \). Strong positive evidence, about 88% sure of "yes."

    \( z = -1 \): \( e^{-(-1)} = e^{1} = 2.718 \). Then \( \sigma(-1) = \frac{1}{1 + 2.718} = \frac{1}{3.718} \approx 0.269 \). Negative evidence, about 27% — i.e. ~73% sure of "no."

    Sanity check: as \( z \) went −1 → 0 → 2, the probability climbed 0.27 → 0.50 → 0.88. More evidence, more confidence, smoothly. Good.

    05 · the lab — drive the dimmer

    Bend the curve. Move the boundary. Flip a point.

    This is the dimmer switch, live. Slide \( w \) to make the S steep or gentle, slide \( b \) to drag it left or right. The orange line is the decision boundary (where \( wx + b = 0 \)); watch each point flip its predicted class as it crosses \( p = 0.5 \). Don't just wiggle it — run the missions.

    LAB sigmoid & the decision boundary

    Each mission is something you make happen in the widget, then verify with your eyes (or your boss companion).

    • Park the decision boundary at a target x Slide b until the 0.5 crossing sits at a specific x you pick (say x = 3). The boundary lives at x = −b/w.
    • Make the curve STEEP, then GENTLE Crank w large → a near-vertical cliff (decisive, almost a hard switch). Shrink w toward 0 → a lazy ramp (everything hovers near 0.5).
    • Flip a point's predicted class Pick a point near the boundary and move w or b until it crosses 0.5 — its class label should switch.
    • Break it on purpose Set w negative. What happens to "more evidence = more yes"? Explain why the curve now runs backward.
    What "steep w" actually means

    \( w \) is the gain on the dimmer. Big \( |w| \) = the curve goes from ~0 to ~1 over a tiny range of \( x \): the model is decisive, almost a hard switch, confident everywhere except a razor-thin no-man's-land. Small \( w \) = a gentle ramp: wishy-washy, most points sit near 0.5. Negative \( w \) flips the orientation — now bigger \( x \) means lower probability. The boundary location is set by \( b \) (specifically \( x = -b/w \)); the boundary sharpness is set by \( w \).

    06 · how wrong is a probability?

    Confident and wrong should hurt. A lot.

    To train this thing we need to measure error. In Module 3 we used squared distance \( (\hat{y}-y)^2 \). For probabilities that is a weak punishment — being 99% sure of the wrong answer barely registers. We want a loss that is near zero when you're confidently right, and that explodes toward infinity when you're confidently wrong. That is how a model learns humility.

    Before the formula, predict the shape of the punishment:

    The true label is 1. Your model said \( \hat{y} = 0.01 \) — just 1% on the thing that actually happened. Log loss penalises you by \( -\ln(\hat{y}) \). Roughly how big is that penalty?

    \( -\ln(0.01) \approx 4.6 \). Push your wrong confidence to \( \hat{y} = 0.001 \) and the penalty jumps to \( \approx 6.9 \); at \( 10^{-6} \) it's \( \approx 13.8 \). The logarithm has a vertical wall at 0 — as your wrong-confidence approaches certainty, the loss approaches infinity. Meanwhile if you'd said 0.99 and been right, \( -\ln(0.99) \approx 0.01 \), almost free. That brutal asymmetry is the entire point of log loss.

    The formula for a single example with true label \( y \in \{0,1\} \) and predicted probability \( \hat{y} \):

    \[ L(\hat{y}, y) = -\big[\, y \ln(\hat{y}) + (1 - y)\ln(1 - \hat{y}) \,\big] \]

    y the true label, 0 or 1. ŷ your predicted probability of class 1. ln natural log (base \( e \)). The \( y \) and \( (1-y) \) are an on/off switch: only one term ever survives.

    It looks like two terms but only one is ever live. If \( y = 1 \), then \( (1-y) = 0 \) deletes the second term and the loss is \( -\ln(\hat{y}) \) — "how little probability did you give to yes?" If \( y = 0 \), the first term vanishes and the loss is \( -\ln(1-\hat{y}) \) — "how little did you give to no?" Average \( L \) over all examples to get the total cost gradient descent minimises.

    Do it by hand. Your model predicts \( \hat{y} = 0.9 \). Compute the loss in both worlds before you peek.

    Check my log-loss arithmetic

    True label \( y = 1 \) (confident and right): loss \( = -\ln(0.9) = -(-0.105) \approx 0.105 \). Small — gently nudged, because you were confident and correct but not perfectly certain.

    True label \( y = 0 \) (confident and wrong): loss \( = -\ln(1 - 0.9) = -\ln(0.1) = -(-2.302) \approx 2.302 \). More than twenty times bigger for the exact same prediction. The model is hammered for being sure of the wrong thing.

    0.105 versus 2.302 — that gap is log loss teaching the model to be confident only when it has earned it.

    07 · the optimiser is free

    No new optimiser. Module 4, again.

    Here is the delightful part. To train, we minimise the average log loss with gradient descent — the exact procedure from Module 4. We just need the gradients (the slopes telling us how to nudge \( w \) and \( b \)). After the calculus settles, they come out identical in shape to linear regression's:

    \[ \frac{\partial L}{\partial w} = (\hat{y} - y)\,x, \qquad \frac{\partial L}{\partial b} = (\hat{y} - y) \]

    (ŷ − y) the prediction error. Weight gradient = error × input. Bias gradient = error alone (its "input" is the constant 1). The only change from Module 3 is that \( \hat{y} \) is now \( \sigma(wx+b) \), not raw \( wx+b \).

    So the update rule is the one you already know cold:

    \[ w \leftarrow w - \alpha\,\frac{\partial L}{\partial w}, \qquad b \leftarrow b - \alpha\,\frac{\partial L}{\partial b} \]

    α (alpha) the learning rate, a small positive step size. ← means "becomes." Same loop, same minus sign, same robot-in-fog from Module 4.

    Logistic regression is linear regression wearing a sigmoid hat and a log-loss coat. Now find the bug a beginner planted in the loop.

    This trainer runs without errors, but the loss climbs every step instead of falling. Read it and spot the bug before you open the fix.

    import numpy as np

def sigmoid(z):
    return 1.0 / (1.0 + np.exp(-z))

X = np.array([[1.,1.],[1.5,.5],[.5,1.5],[1.,2.],
              [4.,4.],[4.5,3.5],[3.5,4.5],[4.,5.]])
y = np.array([0,0,0,0,1,1,1,1], dtype=float)
w, b, alpha, n = np.zeros(2), 0.0, 0.1, len(y)

for step in range(2001):
    y_hat = sigmoid(X @ w + b)
    error = y_hat - y
    grad_w = (X.T @ error) / n
    grad_b = error.mean()
    w = w + alpha * grad_w        # <-- something is wrong on this line
    b = b + alpha * grad_b        # <-- and on this one
    Show the bug & why

    The signs are flipped. w = w + alpha * grad_w walks uphill — it climbs the loss. The gradient points toward higher cost, so to go down you must subtract it: w = w - alpha * grad_w and b = b - alpha * grad_b. This is the identical sign trap from Module 4: one flipped minus is the whole difference between a model that learns and one that screams off to infinity.

    08 · the whole thing, from scratch

    Predict the output, then run it.

    The corrected, complete trainer on a tiny 2D dataset: blue points (class 0) low-left, green points (class 1) high-right. Sigmoid, log loss, plain gradient descent. Before you run it — predict: what loss does it start at when every weight is zero, before any learning?

    My guess for the starting loss

    With w = 0, b = 0, every \( z = 0 \), so every \( \hat{y} = \sigma(0) = 0.5 \). The model guesses 0.5 for everything, and log loss of a constant 0.5 is \( -\ln(0.5) \approx 0.693 \). That is the "I know nothing" baseline — if your loss ever sits above 0.693, your model is worse than a coin flip.

    import numpy as np

# --- tiny 2D dataset: 4 points class 0 (lower-left), 4 points class 1 (upper-right)
X = np.array([
    [1.0, 1.0], [1.5, 0.5], [0.5, 1.5], [1.0, 2.0],   # class 0
    [4.0, 4.0], [4.5, 3.5], [3.5, 4.5], [4.0, 5.0],   # class 1
])
y = np.array([0, 0, 0, 0, 1, 1, 1, 1], dtype=float)

def sigmoid(z):
    return 1.0 / (1.0 + np.exp(-z))

def log_loss(y_true, y_hat):
    eps = 1e-12  # clip so we never take log(0)
    y_hat = np.clip(y_hat, eps, 1 - eps)
    return -np.mean(y_true * np.log(y_hat) + (1 - y_true) * np.log(1 - y_hat))

# --- parameters: one weight per feature, plus a bias
w = np.zeros(2)     # [w1, w2]
b = 0.0
alpha = 0.1         # learning rate
n = len(y)

for step in range(2001):
    z = X @ w + b               # linear score for every example
    y_hat = sigmoid(z)          # squash to probabilities
    error = y_hat - y           # (y_hat - y), the shared gradient piece
    grad_w = (X.T @ error) / n  # average gradient for the weights
    grad_b = error.mean()       # average gradient for the bias
    w -= alpha * grad_w         # gradient descent step (note the MINUS)
    b -= alpha * grad_b
    if step % 500 == 0:
        print(f"step {step:4d}  loss {log_loss(y, y_hat):.4f}  w={np.round(w,3)} b={b:.3f}")

# --- evaluate
probs = sigmoid(X @ w + b)
preds = (probs >= 0.5).astype(int)
print("predicted classes:", preds)
print("accuracy:", (preds == y).mean())

    The loss starts near 0.693 (your predicted \( -\ln 0.5 \) baseline) and falls steadily toward roughly 0.05 as the weights grow. The last line reports accuracy: 1.0 — all eight points correct. The boundary is wherever X @ w + b == 0; both learned weights are positive, so it's the diagonal splitting lower-left from upper-right. And notice: no new optimiser was written. The error = y_hat - y trick is the same gradient shape from Module 4.

    09 · the three-line version & going multiclass

    scikit-learn, and beyond yes/no.

    Once you understand the mechanics, the library does all of it — and hands you predict_proba so you can read calibrated probabilities, not just hard labels. Binary Iris: is the flower setosa, or not? Predict the first row of predict_proba before running — setosa is famously easy to separate, so expect near-certainty.

    from sklearn.datasets import load_iris
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split

iris = load_iris()
X = iris.data                       # 4 features per flower
y = (iris.target == 0).astype(int) # 1 if species 'setosa', else 0

X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(
    X, y, test_size=0.3, random_state=42)

model = LogisticRegression()
model.fit(X_train, y_train)         # gradient-based optimisation, under the hood

print("test accuracy:", model.score(X_test, y_test))

# probabilities for the first 3 test flowers: P(class 0), P(class 1)
print(model.predict_proba(X_test[:3]).round(3))
print("hard predictions:", model.predict(X_test[:3]))

    Prints test accuracy: 1.0, then a small table where each row is two numbers summing to 1 — e.g. [0.998 0.002] meaning "99.8% not-setosa, 0.2% setosa." .predict just applies the 0.5 threshold to those probabilities for you. Same idea as your from-scratch loop, fewer keystrokes, plus battle-tested numerical care.

    Three or more classes: softmax

    What about ten digits, or all three Iris species? Generalise the sigmoid to softmax. Instead of one score \( z \), compute one score \( z_k \) per class \( k \), then turn the whole bundle into probabilities that are all positive and sum to 1:

    \[ P(\text{class}=k) = \frac{e^{z_k}}{\sum_{j} e^{z_j}} \]

    zk the score for class \( k \). The denominator adds \( e^{z_j} \) over every class \( j \), normalising so the probabilities sum to 1. Exponentiate every score (forces positive), then divide each by the total.

    The biggest score gets the biggest probability; the chosen class is the highest one. The matching loss is categorical cross-entropy — the same "negative log of the probability you gave the true class" idea, extended to many classes. scikit-learn does this automatically: call LogisticRegression on a target with three labels and it runs the multiclass version. You'll meet softmax again as the last layer of classification neural networks in Module 10.

    Lock this in. The sigmoid you bent in the lab is the 2-class special case of softmax. You will see softmax again in Module 10 as the final layer of every classification neural net — same exponentiate-then-normalise, just wider. The trap to dodge: sigmoid is for binary (one probability); softmax is for multiclass (a probability per class). A sigmoid bolted onto ten classes is a bug, not a shortcut.
    10 · cold recall — no scrolling back

    Pull it out of your own head.

    Re-reading feels like learning but mostly isn't. Retrieving is. Answer each in your head, then flip. Can't? That's your signal to scroll up — but try first.

    Why can't a plain straight line predict yes/no probabilities?
    tap to flip
    A line climbs forever — it returns nonsense like 4.7 or −2.3, outside [0,1], and outliers tilt the whole boundary. The sigmoid squashes evidence into a value that can't escape 0 to 1.
    What is \( \sigma(0) \), and why?
    tap to flip
    0.5. At \( z=0 \), \( e^{0}=1 \), so \( \sigma = \frac{1}{1+1} \). Zero evidence = coin flip. That's also where the decision boundary sits.
    Why log loss instead of squared error?
    tap to flip
    Log loss explodes toward infinity for confident-and-wrong predictions (the log has a wall at 0) and is near-zero for confident-and-right. Squared error barely punishes overconfidence.
    What's new in the training loop vs. Module 4?
    tap to flip
    Nothing in the optimiser. Same \( w \leftarrow w - \alpha\cdot\text{grad} \), same error = y_hat - y gradient shape. The only change: \( \hat{y} = \sigma(wx+b) \) instead of raw \( wx+b \).
    Sigmoid vs. softmax — when each?
    tap to flip
    Sigmoid for binary (one probability). Softmax for 3+ classes (a probability per class, summing to 1). Softmax with 2 classes reduces to the sigmoid.
    11 · boss fight
    🤖 Boss fight — with your Claude companion

    A box of "suggested prompts" is lazy. Here's a real challenge loop. Paste it to your Claude Code companion and don't move on until you beat it cold — by hand, no calculator from them.

    You are quizzing me on logistic regression. Run an adaptive loop. Round 1: give me a w, b, and an x, and I will compute z, then sigmoid(z) by hand, then say the predicted class and where the decision boundary is. Round 2: give me a true label y and a predicted probability, and I will compute the log loss by hand and tell you whether the model was confident-and-right or confident-and-wrong. Grade each answer, show me exactly where I slipped if I did, then escalate — make the next one harder (negative z, probabilities near 0 or 1 where the loss explodes, a case where the 0.5 threshold is the wrong call). Keep going until I get two rounds in a row perfect, then make me explain in words why log loss punishes confident-wrong far more than squared error would.

    You pass this module when you can compute \( \sigma(z) \) and a log loss in your head, locate the decision boundary, and explain — using the dimmer switch and the robot-in-fog, not a memorised formula — why confident-and-wrong gets hammered and why training reused Module 4 unchanged.

    12 · spar a few more

    Extra reps, answers hidden.

    1. Compute \( \sigma(z) \) by hand for \( z = 1 \) and \( z = -2 \) (use \( e \approx 2.718 \)). Which prediction is more confident, and in which direction?
      Show solution

      \( z = 1 \): \( e^{-1} = 1/2.718 \approx 0.368 \), so \( \sigma(1) = \frac{1}{1.368} \approx 0.731 \) — about 73% for class 1.

      \( z = -2 \): \( e^{2} \approx 7.389 \), so \( \sigma(-2) = \frac{1}{8.389} \approx 0.119 \) — about 12% for class 1, i.e. ~88% for class 0.

      \( z = -2 \) is more confident (0.119 is further from 0.5 than 0.731 is) and points toward class 0.

    2. A model predicts \( \hat{y} = 0.6 \). Compute the log loss if \( y = 1 \), and again if \( y = 0 \). Which is larger, and why?
      Show solution

      \( y = 1 \): loss \( = -\ln(0.6) \approx 0.511 \).

      \( y = 0 \): loss \( = -\ln(1 - 0.6) = -\ln(0.4) \approx 0.916 \).

      The \( y=0 \) case is larger: the model leaned the wrong way (only 0.4 on the correct class 0), so it's penalised more than when it leaned right.

    3. With \( w = 2 \), \( b = -4 \) for a single feature \( x \): where is the decision boundary? Predict the class for \( x = 1 \) and \( x = 3 \).
      Show solution

      Boundary: \( 2x - 4 = 0 \Rightarrow x = 2 \). For \( x \gt 2 \), \( z \gt 0 \) so class 1; for \( x \lt 2 \), class 0.

      \( x = 1 \): \( z = 2(1)-4 = -2 \), \( \sigma(-2) \approx 0.12 \) → class 0. \( x = 3 \): \( z = 2(3)-4 = 2 \), \( \sigma(2) \approx 0.88 \) → class 1.

    4. The imbalance trap. 5% of transactions are fraud. A model always predicts "not fraud." What's its accuracy, why is it terrible, and what would you do instead?
      Show solution

      Accuracy = 95% (right on every legit transaction, which are 95% of the data) yet it catches zero fraud — useless for the actual goal. Fix: lower the threshold well below 0.5 so suspicious transactions get flagged (more false alarms, more fraud caught), and judge with recall and precision (or area under the precision–recall curve), not raw accuracy. You'll formalise these in Module 6.

    Where this returns. Module 6 (Model Evaluation) is where the 0.5 threshold and "accuracy lies on imbalanced data" come back to bite — you'll learn precision, recall, and AUC there. Module 7 (Feature Engineering) is where "scale your features first" matters. And Module 10 puts a softmax on the end of a neural net, where this exact math becomes the final layer. One squash, three modules.
    Module 6

    Model Evaluation: Overfitting, Bias-Variance, Cross-Validation, Regularization

    Your model just scored 99.5% accuracy. You ship it. It catches zero criminals.

    Here's the trap. You built a fraud detector. Fraud is 0.5% of all transactions. A model that does literally nothing — that screams "not fraud!" at every single transaction — also scores 99.5%. Same number. One of you catches crooks; the other is a return False wearing a lab coat.

    This module is one long argument that a single number can lie to you, and a toolkit for catching it in the act: how to tell whether a model learned or just memorized, how to measure the thing you actually care about, and how to tune a model so it survives data it has never seen. Don't skip the prediction cards — the whole point is to commit to a guess before you see the answer. The gap is where it sticks.

    01 · the cheater's tell

    A model that aces the practice exam and bombs the real one

    Every model has exactly one job: do well on data it has never seen before. That's what "learning" means. So before training, you hide some data:

    • The training set — the examples the model is allowed to study.
    • The test set — locked in a drawer, used once at the very end to grade it. The final exam it never got to peek at.

    Now the gap between the two scores becomes a confession:

    • Underfit — bad on training and test. Too dumb to see the pattern. Tracing a curvy river with a single straight ruler.
    • Overfit — great on training, bad on test. It memorized the noise. The student who learned last year's exam word-for-word and melts when the wording changes.
    • Just right — good on training, nearly as good on test. Small gap.

    You crank a polynomial up to degree 9 so it threads through every single training point — training error basically zero. What does it do on the test set?

    "Fits the training data perfectly" and "generalizes" become opposites once you go too far. To pass through every noisy point the curve has to thrash up and down between them — and that thrashing is pure invention. New points don't sit on the noise the curve memorized, so they land in the gaps where the curve predicts nonsense. Training error keeps dropping as degree rises; test error bottoms out in the middle and then climbs. That U-shape in the test curve is the signature of overfitting, and you're about to watch it live.

    02 · the lab

    Find the U with your own hands

    Below is the live version of that argument. Drag the polynomial degree from 1 to 9 and watch two curves: training error (always falling) and test error (the one that matters). "Resample data" draws fresh noise — use it to prove the pattern is real, not a fluke of one dataset. Don't just wiggle the slider. Run the three missions.

    LAB overfitting playground

    Goal: see underfit, overfit, and the sweet spot in the same widget — and feel where the test curve turns around.

    • Find the degree with the lowest test errorIt's usually in the middle, not the max. That degree is "just right." Write it down.
    • Force an overfit: push the degree up until training error is near zero but test error climbsWatch the curve sprout wiggles between the points. Training looks amazing, test gets worse. That's the lie.
    • Force an underfit: drop to degree 1 (a straight line)Now both errors are high. Too simple to catch the curve. Same data, opposite failure.
    What should I have seen?
    Degree 1: a straight line missing the bend — high train AND test error (underfit). Mid degrees (often 3–4): both errors low and close together (the sweet spot). High degrees (8–9): training error crashes toward zero while test error shoots back up as the curve grows wild oscillations between the points (overfit). Resampling redraws the noise but the U-shape in the test curve survives — that's the proof it's a real phenomenon, not one unlucky dataset.
    03 · why you can't just always go flexible

    The dartboard

    So if more flexibility lowers training error, why not always crank it up? Because flexibility cuts both ways, and the cleanest picture for this is a dartboard. Imagine training your model many times on slightly different samples of data. Each trained model is one dart thrown at the bullseye (the true answer).

    An overfit model is like which dartboard pattern?

    C — that scatter is variance. An overfit model overreacts to whatever noise was in its training sample, so tiny changes in the data swing it dramatically: the darts land everywhere. Pattern A is the opposite failure — an underfit model is consistently wrong in the same direction (a tight cluster, but off-target). That consistent offset is bias. Pattern B, tight and centered, is the dream you rarely fully get.

    So the two words mean:

    • Bias — how far, on average, your darts land from the bullseye. High bias = consistently off the same way. That's an underfit, too-simple model: same mistake no matter the data.
    • Variance — how spread out the darts are from each other. High variance = scatter that depends on which sample you happened to draw. That's an overfit model.

    Green = the true target. Blue dots = models trained on different samples. Bias = distance from center; variance = spread.

    The tradeoff: make a model more flexible and bias drops (it can fit more shapes) but variance rises (it overreacts to noise). Make it simpler and the reverse. The error you actually feel splits into three pieces:

    \[ \text{Expected test error} = \text{Bias}^2 + \text{Variance} + \text{irreducible noise} \]

    Bias systematic wrongness — the same mistake every time. Variance sensitivity to which training sample you got. irreducible noise randomness in the world no model can ever predict (a fair coin flip). The sweet spot is the complexity that minimizes the first two — exactly the bottom of the U you found in the lab.

    04 · don't trust one split

    Cross-validation: the test set, but five times

    One train-test split has a quiet flaw: your score depends on which rows happened to land in the test pile. Unlucky split, you trash a good model. Lucky split, you over-praise a bad one. On small data this luck-of-the-draw can swing the number a lot.

    k-fold cross-validation refuses to rely on one split. Chop the data into \( k \) equal chunks (folds; a common choice is \( k = 5 \)). Train and test \( k \) times. Each round one fold is the test set and the other \( k-1 \) folds are training. Every row gets to be a test row exactly once.

    5-fold CV: every fold is the test set once. Average the 5 scores for a stable estimate.

    \[ \text{CV score} = \frac{1}{k} \sum_{i=1}^{k} s_i \]

    k the number of folds. s_i the score (say accuracy) when fold \( i \) was held out for testing. The final score is just the average of the \( k \) rounds.

    Bonus: the spread of those \( k \) scores (their standard deviation) is its own warning light. A huge spread means the model is unstable — high variance — even if the average looks fine.

    05 · the four buckets

    Classification metrics: where accuracy goes to die

    For a yes/no classifier every prediction lands in one of four buckets — the confusion matrix. Using fraud as "positive":

    • True Positive (TP): predicted positive, was positive. (Caught real fraud.)
    • False Positive (FP): predicted positive, was negative. (Declined a legit purchase — false alarm.)
    • False Negative (FN): predicted negative, was positive. (Missed real fraud.)
    • True Negative (TN): predicted negative, was negative. (Correctly let a legit purchase through.)

    Three metrics fall out of those four counts, and each answers a different question:

    \[ \text{Accuracy} = \frac{TP + TN}{TP + TN + FP + FN} \]

    "Of all predictions, what fraction were correct?" Simple — and a liar on imbalanced data (remember the fraud opener).

    \[ \text{Precision} = \frac{TP}{TP + FP} \]

    "When the model says positive, how often is it right?" High precision = few false alarms. Care about it when a false positive is expensive — your real card getting declined at a restaurant.

    \[ \text{Recall} = \frac{TP}{TP + FN} \]

    (a.k.a. sensitivity) "Of all the actual positives, how many did we catch?" High recall = few misses. Care about it when a false negative is dangerous — a cancer screen that misses a real tumor.

    \[ F_1 = 2 \cdot \frac{\text{Precision} \cdot \text{Recall}}{\text{Precision} + \text{Recall}} \]

    One number balancing the two. It's the harmonic mean, which punishes the case where one is high and the other is low — you can't fake it by maxing only one. The leading 2 makes it equal 1 when both are perfect.

    The hidden knob: the threshold. Most classifiers don't output yes/no — they output a probability, like 0.83. You pick a threshold (default 0.5): positive if above it. Lower the threshold and you catch more positives (recall up) but raise false alarms (precision down). The ROC curve plots true-positive rate vs false-positive rate as you sweep the threshold; AUC (area under it) collapses the whole curve into one number from 0.5 (random) to 1.0 (perfect), measuring how well the model ranks positives above negatives regardless of any one threshold.

    06 · do it by hand

    Worked example — predict, then compute

    A spam classifier is tested on 100 emails ("spam" = positive):

    • TP = 40 (spam correctly caught)
    • FP = 10 (good email wrongly marked spam)
    • FN = 20 (spam that slipped through)
    • TN = 30 (good email correctly delivered)

    Before computing anything: precision is \(40/50 = 0.80\) and recall is \(40/60 \approx 0.667\). Where will F1 land?

    The harmonic mean always sits at or below the plain average, and it leans toward the weaker of the two. That's the entire point: F1 won't let you brag about precision while recall quietly tanks. Below it works out to 0.727 — under the arithmetic mean of 0.733, tugged down by the 0.667 recall.

    Now the arithmetic, every fraction shown. Try each yourself first, then check.

    \[ \text{Accuracy} = \frac{40 + 30}{40+30+10+20} = \frac{70}{100} = 0.70 \]

    70% of emails classified correctly.

    \[ \text{Precision} = \frac{40}{40+10} = \frac{40}{50} = 0.80 \]

    When this filter says "spam," it's right 80% of the time.

    \[ \text{Recall} = \frac{40}{40+20} = \frac{40}{60} = 0.6\overline{6} \approx 0.667 \]

    We caught ~67% of the spam; the other 33% slipped into inboxes.

    \[ F_1 = 2 \cdot \frac{0.80 \times 0.667}{0.80 + 0.667} = 2 \cdot \frac{0.5336}{1.467} = 2 \cdot 0.3637 \approx 0.727 \]

    Accuracy 0.70, precision 0.80, recall 0.667, F1 0.727. F1 sits between precision and recall, pulled toward the smaller — exactly the balance you predicted.

    07 · when you predict a number

    Regression metrics: MAE vs RMSE

    Predicting a price or a temperature instead of a class? Two metrics dominate. Let \( y_i \) be the true value, \( \hat{y}_i \) ("y-hat") the prediction, \( n \) the number of examples.

    \[ \text{MAE} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n} |y_i - \hat{y}_i| \]

    MAE (Mean Absolute Error) average size of the mistakes; an over- and under-prediction of the same size count equally. Forgiving of outliers, easy to say out loud ("on average we're off by $4,200").

    \[ \text{RMSE} = \sqrt{\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n} (y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2} \]

    RMSE (Root Mean Squared Error) square the errors first, average, then square-root back to original units. The squaring makes big mistakes hurt disproportionately — the metric to watch when one large error is much worse than several small ones.

    You met squared error in Module 3 — Linear Regression as the thing you minimized; here it comes back as the thing you report.

    Model A's errors are [2, 2, 2, 2, 2]. Model B's are [0, 0, 0, 0, 10]. Both have MAE = 2.0. Which metric exposes Model B's one giant mistake?

    MAE averages raw error sizes, so it treats five mistakes of 2 the same as one mistake of 10 — both give 2.0. RMSE squares first: \( \sqrt{(0+0+0+0+100)/5} = \sqrt{20} \approx 4.47 \) for B versus \( \sqrt{20/5} = 2.0 \) for A. The squaring is what makes the single outlier scream. Pick RMSE when a rare huge error is the thing you're scared of.

    08 · the lever against overfitting

    Regularization: tax the loud weights

    Overfitting usually shows up as huge weights. To thread through every noisy point, a model cranks its coefficients to extremes — that's where the wild wiggles in the lab came from. Regularization adds a penalty to the loss for being too loud, forcing a trade between fitting the data and keeping weights small.

    \[ \text{Loss}_{\text{ridge}} = \underbrace{\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2}_{\text{fit the data}} + \underbrace{\lambda \sum_{j=1}^{p} w_j^2}_{\text{stay small}} \]

    L2 / Ridge. w_j the \( j \)-th weight, p the number of weights, λ ("lambda") the regularization strength you choose. \( \lambda = 0 \) is plain regression; as \( \lambda \) grows, the penalty dominates and weights are squeezed toward zero — simpler model, lower variance, higher bias. Ridge shrinks smoothly but rarely hits exactly zero.

    \[ \text{Loss}_{\text{lasso}} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2 + \lambda \sum_{j=1}^{p} |w_j| \]

    L1 / Lasso. Same idea, but it penalizes the absolute values. The kink at zero lets it drive some weights exactly to zero — deleting those features outright. That makes Lasso a built-in feature selector, handy when you suspect many features are useless.

    And you pick \( \lambda \) the proper way — with cross-validation — not by eyeballing it. Bias-variance, CV, and regularization aren't separate topics; they're one loop: CV measures the U, \( \lambda \) slides you along it.

    09 · find the bug

    This CV code looks careful. It cheats.

    A diligent beginner scaled their features (good!) and cross-validated (good!). Their CV score comes back suspiciously high, and the model later flops in production. The code runs without errors. Find the bug before opening the answer.

    import numpy as np
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_score

X, y = load_my_data()

scaler = StandardScaler()
X = scaler.fit_transform(X)          # <-- scale EVERYTHING up front, before CV

clf = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)
scores = cross_val_score(clf, X, y, cv=5, scoring="f1")
print(scores.mean())                 # looks great... too great
    Show the bug & the fix

    This is data leakage. scaler.fit_transform(X) computes the mean and standard deviation from the entire dataset — including the rows that will later sit in each CV test fold. So when cross_val_score holds out a fold to "test" on, that fold already shaped the scaling. Information about the test data leaked into training, and the score comes back optimistically high. In production, where genuinely unseen data can't leak backward in time, performance drops.

    Fix: put the scaler in a Pipeline so it is refit on each fold's training portion only:

    from sklearn.pipeline import make_pipeline

pipe = make_pipeline(StandardScaler(), LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000))
scores = cross_val_score(pipe, X, y, cv=5, scoring="f1")  # honest now
print(scores.mean())

    Rule of thumb: any step that learns from the data (scaling, imputation, feature selection) must live inside the CV loop, never before it. More on this in Module 7 — Feature Engineering and Preprocessing.

    10 · build it from scratch

    The metrics are just four counts and arithmetic

    No magic. Predict the printed line in your head before you run this — you already did the math above, so you know the four numbers.

    def classification_metrics(tp, fp, fn, tn):
    accuracy = (tp + tn) / (tp + fp + fn + tn)
    precision = tp / (tp + fp) if (tp + fp) > 0 else 0.0
    recall = tp / (tp + fn) if (tp + fn) > 0 else 0.0
    if precision + recall > 0:
        f1 = 2 * precision * recall / (precision + recall)
    else:
        f1 = 0.0
    return accuracy, precision, recall, f1

acc, prec, rec, f1 = classification_metrics(tp=40, fp=10, fn=20, tn=30)
print(f"accuracy={acc:.3f} precision={prec:.3f} recall={rec:.3f} f1={f1:.3f}")

# K-fold split indices by hand (no libraries)
def kfold_indices(n_samples, k):
    indices = list(range(n_samples))
    fold_size = n_samples // k
    folds = []
    start = 0
    for i in range(k):
        # last fold absorbs any remainder
        end = start + fold_size if i < k - 1 else n_samples
        test_idx = indices[start:end]
        train_idx = indices[:start] + indices[end:]
        folds.append((train_idx, test_idx))
        start = end
    return folds

for fold_num, (train_idx, test_idx) in enumerate(kfold_indices(10, k=5), 1):
    print(f"Fold {fold_num}: test={test_idx} train={train_idx}")

    It prints accuracy=0.700 precision=0.800 recall=0.667 f1=0.727 — matching your hand calc exactly. The k-fold loop prints five rows; fold 1 tests on indices [0, 1] and trains on [2..9], fold 2 tests on [2, 3], and so on, so every index appears in a test set exactly once. (In practice you'd shuffle the indices first so the folds aren't biased by row order.)

    The library way

    scikit-learn does all of it in a few lines. Before running: cross_val_score returns one number per fold. How many numbers will the first print show? (Five — cv=5.) And as alpha grows, which loses more weights to zero, Ridge or Lasso?

    import numpy as np
from sklearn.datasets import make_classification, make_regression
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression, Ridge, Lasso
from sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_score, train_test_split
from sklearn.metrics import confusion_matrix, classification_report

# --- classification: cross-validation + confusion matrix ---
X, y = make_classification(n_samples=400, n_features=8, weights=[0.7, 0.3],
                           random_state=0)
clf = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)

scores = cross_val_score(clf, X, y, cv=5, scoring="f1")
print(f"5-fold F1: {scores}")
print(f"mean={scores.mean():.3f}  std={scores.std():.3f}")

X_tr, X_te, y_tr, y_te = train_test_split(X, y, test_size=0.25, random_state=0)
clf.fit(X_tr, y_tr)
pred = clf.predict(X_te)
print("Confusion matrix [[TN FP] [FN TP]]:")
print(confusion_matrix(y_te, pred))
print(classification_report(y_te, pred, digits=3))

# --- regression: Ridge vs Lasso as lambda (alpha) grows ---
Xr, yr = make_regression(n_samples=200, n_features=20, n_informative=5,
                         noise=10.0, random_state=0)
for alpha in [0.1, 1.0, 10.0]:
    ridge = Ridge(alpha=alpha).fit(Xr, yr)
    lasso = Lasso(alpha=alpha).fit(Xr, yr)
    n_zero = int(np.sum(np.abs(lasso.coef_) < 1e-8))
    print(f"alpha={alpha:>4}: ridge||w||={np.linalg.norm(ridge.coef_):.1f}  "
          f"lasso zeros={n_zero}/20")

    scikit-learn calls the regularization strength alpha (our \( \lambda \)). The CV block prints five F1 scores plus their mean and standard deviation — far more trustworthy than any single split. As alpha grows, Ridge's total weight magnitude shrinks, and Lasso zeroes out more of the 20 features (it should keep roughly the 5 truly informative ones) — L1's automatic feature selection in action.

    11 · pull it from your own head

    Cold recall — no scrolling back

    Re-reading feels like learning and mostly isn't. Retrieving is. Answer each in your head, then flip. If you can't, that's your signal to scroll up — but try first.

    Train score 0.99, test score 0.71. Name the problem and one fix.
    tap to flip
    Overfitting — the big train-test gap. Fix: regularize (raise λ), simplify the model, or get more data.
    Precision vs recall — one line each.
    tap to flip
    Precision: when I say positive, am I right? TP/(TP+FP). Recall: of all real positives, did I catch them? TP/(TP+FN).
    Underfit vs overfit, in bias/variance terms?
    tap to flip
    Underfit = high bias, low variance (too simple, same mistake every time). Overfit = low bias, high variance (too wiggly, swings with the sample).
    Why does one train-test split mislead, and how does k-fold help?
    tap to flip
    One split's score depends on which rows landed in test (luck). k-fold tests on every row once and averages the k scores — stable estimate, plus the spread flags instability.
    Ridge vs Lasso — the one practical difference?
    tap to flip
    Both shrink weights as λ grows. Lasso (L1) can drive weights exactly to zero → built-in feature selection. Ridge (L2) shrinks smoothly but rarely to zero.
    12 · boss fight
    🤖 Boss fight — with your Claude companion

    A list of "suggested prompts" is lazy. Here's a real challenge loop. Paste it to your Claude Code companion and don't move on until you beat it cold — by hand, no calculator from me.

    You are drilling me on model evaluation. Give me ONE confusion matrix with specific TP/FP/FN/TN values. I will compute accuracy, precision, recall, and F1 BY HAND and tell you my four numbers. Grade each, show the exact fraction where I slipped, then escalate: next make the data imbalanced and ask me whether accuracy is trustworthy and why; then give me a real-world scenario (cancer screen, spam filter, fraud) and make me choose precision vs recall and defend it; then hand me two regression error lists and have me compute MAE and RMSE and say which to report. Keep going, raising difficulty, until I get two full rounds perfect in a row. Do not reveal an answer until after I commit to mine.

    You pass when you can read a confusion matrix and produce precision, recall, and F1 without the formulas in front of you, AND explain out loud why 99.5% accuracy can still mean a useless model.

    13 · extra reps

    A few more, with solutions hidden

    1. A model produces TP = 90, FP = 30, FN = 10, TN = 870. Compute accuracy, precision, recall, F1. Is accuracy a fair summary here?
      Show solution
      Accuracy = (90 + 870)/1000 = 0.960. Precision = 90/120 = 0.75. Recall = 90/100 = 0.90. F1 = 2·(0.75·0.90)/(0.75+0.90) ≈ 0.818. Accuracy looks great (0.96) but only 100 of 1000 are positive — the easy negatives inflate it. Precision/recall/F1 are the honest picture.
    2. 12 rows, 4-fold CV, no shuffling. List the test indices per fold, and say how many times each row is used for training.
      Show solution
      12/4 = 3 rows per fold. Fold 1 test = [0,1,2], Fold 2 = [3,4,5], Fold 3 = [6,7,8], Fold 4 = [9,10,11]. Each row is a test row exactly once, so it's a training row in the other k−1 = 3 rounds.
    3. For a hospital screening test, prioritize precision or recall? What happens to each as you lower the threshold?
      Show solution
      Prioritize recall: a false negative tells a sick patient they're healthy — potentially fatal — so catch as many true cases as possible, accepting more false alarms (follow-up tests rule those out). Lowering the threshold predicts "positive" more readily: recall goes up, precision goes down.
    4. Train Ridge with λ = 0, then push λ to a very large value. What happens to (a) the weights, (b) bias, (c) variance? At λ → ∞, what does the model predict?
      Show solution
      (a) Weights shrink toward zero as the penalty \( \lambda \sum w_j^2 \) outweighs the fit term. (b) Bias increases — simpler model, less able to follow the data. (c) Variance decreases — tiny weights react less to noise. As λ → ∞ all weights → 0, so the model ignores the features and just predicts the constant mean of the training targets (max bias, near-zero variance).
    Lock this in. The U-shaped test curve you found in the lab is the spine of everything ahead. In Module 7 you'll see why scaling must live inside the CV loop (the leakage bug, formalized). In Module 8+ every model you meet — trees, forests, gradient boosting, neural nets — gets diagnosed with the exact same train-vs-test gap and tuned with the exact same loop: cross-validate to find the bottom of the U, regularize to stay there.
