Different models, identical evaluation playbook.

    You beat this module when you can glance at a 0.99/0.71 train-test split and instantly call "overfit, regularize"; read a confusion matrix and produce precision, recall, and F1 from memory; pick precision vs recall for a real task and defend it; and explain why one train-test split lies where k-fold doesn't.

    Module 7

    Feature Engineering & Preprocessing

    01 · same data, two destinies

    Same rows. Two feature sets. One model wins by a mile.

    Here is the exact same loan dataset, fed to the exact same algorithm, twice. The only difference is what the columns look like when the model sees them.

    LOOK two ways to hand over the same eight rows

    Same applicants. Same labels. Same logistic regression. Read both versions before you scroll.

    # VERSION A — raw, "just throw the spreadsheet at it"
   income   age  home      ->  repaid?
    45000    27  "rent"          ?
   120000    41  "own"           ?
    ...

# VERSION B — same rows, engineered
   income_z  age_z  home_own  home_rent  debt_to_income  ->  repaid?
     -0.81   -0.92      0          1           0.34            ?
      1.40    0.55      1          0           0.11            ?
     ...

    Version A trains slowly, fixates on income because the raw number is huge, chokes on the word "rent" (you can't multiply a weight by "rent"), and silently breaks on the blank cells. Version B converges fast and actually uses age. Nothing about the data changed. Only the features did.

    That gap — same rows, wildly different model — is where amateurs lose and pros win. There is a folk theorem in applied ML: better features beat a fancier algorithm, almost every time. Most beginners spend weeks tuning models. The pros spend that time on the columns. This module is how you join the second group.

    By the end you will scale numbers so a model treats them fairly, build features a linear model can't invent on its own, turn words into numbers without lying to the model, and wire it all into a leak-free scikit-learn Pipeline. But first, a question almost everyone gets wrong.

    02 · commit before you read

    Does scaling even matter? Depends who's asking.

    "Scale your features" gets repeated like gospel. But when it helps depends entirely on the model. Lock in a guess for each.

    You're training gradient descent (linear / logistic regression, a neural net) on income (~45,000) and age (~27). You DON'T scale. What happens?

    When one feature is thousands of times bigger than another, the bowl from Module 4 stretches into a long skinny ravine. Gradient descent zig-zags across the narrow direction instead of walking straight down — slow, fragile, learning-rate-sensitive. Scale both features to a comparable range and the ravine rounds back into a bowl. Straight shot to the bottom. Scaling is the single highest-leverage one-liner for gradient-descent models.

    Now you train a decision tree on the same unscaled income and age. Does scaling help?

    A tree (and a random forest, Module 8) asks yes/no threshold questions: is this value above some cut point? Rescaling moves the cut point by the exact same amount, so the split — and the whole tree — comes out identical. Scaling a tree is wasted effort that can also confuse you about what actually helped. Rule of thumb: scale for gradient-descent and distance-based models; skip it for trees. "Always scale everything" is a beginner reflex, not a rule.

    The recipe analogy. A recipe written half in grams and half in truckloads — "200 grams of flour, 0.0001 truckloads of salt" — is technically correct but impossible to reason about. Convert everything to grams and the relative amounts jump out. Scaling rewrites every feature in comparable units so a gradient-descent model judges importance on the merits, not on the accident of what units someone typed in.

    03 · now the notation

    You felt it. Here's how to actually do it.

    The most common scaler is standardization: rewrite each feature so it has mean 0 and standard deviation 1. The rewritten value is a "z-score" — how many standard deviations a value sits above (+) or below (−) the mean.

    \[ z = \frac{x - \mu}{\sigma} \]

    x one raw value. μ (mu) the mean of the feature — its average. σ (sigma) the standard deviation — how spread out the values are. z the result: distance from the mean, measured in standard deviations.

    In words: subtract the average to recenter on zero, then divide by the spread so the typical distance from zero becomes about 1. Units vanish; shape stays.

    \[ \sigma = \sqrt{\tfrac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}(x_i-\mu)^2} \]

    n how many values. xi the i-th value. ∑ "add up over all values." Inside: each value's distance from the mean, squared (so negatives don't cancel), averaged, then square-rooted back into the original units. This is the population std — exactly what scikit-learn's StandardScaler uses.

    The other scaler you'll meet is min-max normalization, which squeezes every value into [0, 1]:

    \[ x' = \frac{x - x_{\min}}{x_{\max} - x_{\min}} \]

    xmin, xmax the smallest and largest values of the feature. Smallest maps to 0, largest to 1, everything else lands proportionally between.

    Which one? Use min-max when you need a hard bounded range (image pixels, some neural-net inputs). Use standardization when there are outliers or you don't need a 0–1 box — one freak value crushes everything else toward 0 under min-max, but only nudges the mean and spread under standardization.

    04 · you do it first

    Standardize [10, 20, 30] — by hand, before the answer.

    Grab paper. Compute the mean, then the standard deviation, then the three z-scores. Predict all three z-scores before you reveal. (Hint: the list is symmetric — what should the middle z-score be?)

    DO the full standardization, step by step

    Don't peek. Even a wrong number you committed to teaches more than a right one you read.

    Reveal the worked steps

    Step 1 — mean: \( \mu = \frac{10+20+30}{3} = \frac{60}{3} = 20 \).

    Step 2 — squared distances: \( (10-20)^2=100 \), \( (20-20)^2=0 \), \( (30-20)^2=100 \).

    Step 3 — variance (average them): \( \frac{100+0+100}{3} = \frac{200}{3} \approx 66.67 \).

    Step 4 — std: \( \sigma = \sqrt{66.67} \approx 8.165 \).

    Step 5 — z-scores \( z = \frac{x-20}{8.165} \):

    • \( z_{10} = \frac{-10}{8.165} \approx -1.22 \)
    • \( z_{20} = \frac{0}{8.165} = 0 \)
    • \( z_{30} = \frac{10}{8.165} \approx +1.22 \)

    So \( [10, 20, 30] \rightarrow [-1.22,\ 0,\ 1.22] \). The shape is preserved — evenly spaced, symmetric — but the units are gone. These are now "standard deviations from the mean."

    You standardized your training set: income got μ=20, σ=8.165. Now a test value of 40 arrives. What z-score do you give it?

    You learn μ and σ from train only, then freeze them and apply the same numbers to test. The test value 40 becomes ≈ 2.45 — "far above the training average." Recomputing on test (A) or per-set (C) lets test information bleed into your pipeline. That's data leakage (Module 2 — The ML Mindset), and it turns your reported accuracy into a comforting lie. The mantra: fit on train, transform on both.

    05 · words into numbers

    The trap that quietly poisons half of beginner models.

    A column color with values "red", "blue", "green" means nothing to arithmetic. The tempting fix is to map them to 1, 2, 3. Do not.

    You map {red, blue, green} → {1, 2, 3} and feed it to a linear model. What did you just secretly tell the model?

    The model multiplies that number by a weight, so 1/2/3 becomes a real magnitude. You invented an order (green > blue > red) and a spacing that don't exist. The fix is one-hot encoding: give each category its own yes/no column. No category is numerically bigger than another, so no false order leaks in.

    For a categorical feature with \( k \) distinct categories, one-hot makes \( k \) new columns. Each row gets a 1 in its category's column and 0 everywhere else. Your turn — do it on paper first.

    DO one-hot encode [red, blue, red]

    How many columns? What does row 2 look like? Write it before revealing.

    Reveal the encoding

    Distinct categories: {blue, red} — two of them, so two columns, alphabetical:

    rowcoloris_blueis_red
    1red01
    2blue10
    3red01

    One word column became two numeric columns a model can multiply by weights. Add a "green" and you'd get an is_green column too — three columns for three categories.

    Two cousins, used rarely and carefully. Ordinal encoding maps categories to ordered integers — correct only when a real order exists, like shirt size {S, M, L} → {0, 1, 2}. Never for city or color. Target (mean) encoding replaces each category with the average target for that category (e.g. "city = SF" → SF's repayment rate). Powerful for high-cardinality columns, but a leakage magnet: if a row is encoded using its own label, the model peeks at the answer — accuracy looks great until it collapses on new data. If you must, compute the averages with cross-validation folds so a row is never encoded with its own label. As a beginner: prefer one-hot until you know exactly why you'd switch.

    06 · do the model's thinking for it

    A linear model can only add. Hand it the rest.

    A linear model adds up weighted inputs. That's all. It cannot, on its own, multiply two columns or square one. So if the real pattern needs that, you build the column yourself:

    • Interaction feature: if risk is high only when someone is both young and deep in debt, hand the model age × debt as a new column. It could never have formed that product itself.
    • Polynomial feature: squaring a column (age²) lets a straight-line model bend into a curve.
    • Bucketing & combining: turn a messy continuous column into ordered bins, or fold two raw counts into one meaningful count.

    You're doing part of the model's thinking for it. Which is the whole game.

    The same instinct, later. Feature engineering is the classical ancestor of prompt engineering. In both, you can't easily change the model's internals — so you reshape its input to make the right answer easy to produce. Crafting an age × debt column for linear regression and crafting a careful instruction for an LLM are the same move. You'll meet the LLM side in Module 14 — Transformers & LLMs. When you get there, remember: you already know how to do this.
    07 · from scratch, predict the output

    No magic: standardize and one-hot in raw NumPy/pandas.

    Read this. Before you run it, predict the printed numbers. You did the hand math in Beats 4 and 5 — does the code agree with you?

    import numpy as np
import pandas as pd

# --- Standardization from scratch ---
train = np.array([10.0, 20.0, 30.0])
test  = np.array([25.0, 40.0])          # new, unseen values

mu = train.mean()                        # learned from TRAIN only
sigma = train.std()                      # population std (divides by n)
print("mu =", mu, " sigma =", round(sigma, 3))

train_z = (train - mu) / sigma           # transform train
test_z  = (test  - mu) / sigma           # transform test with SAME mu, sigma
print("train standardized:", np.round(train_z, 2))
print("test  standardized:", np.round(test_z, 2))

# --- One-hot encoding from scratch with pandas ---
colors = pd.Series(["red", "blue", "red"], name="color")
onehot = pd.get_dummies(colors, prefix="color").astype(int)
print(onehot)
    Reveal the output & check yourself

    Prints mu = 20.0 sigma = 8.165, then train standardized: [-1.22 0. 1.22] — exactly your hand calc. The test values become [ 0.61 2.45]: 25 is a little above the training mean, 40 is ~2.45 training-std above it. We never recomputed the mean on test. The one-hot block prints two columns, color_blue and color_red, matching your table.

    08 · find the bug

    This code runs clean, reports great accuracy, and is lying to you.

    No error. No crash. The test score looks fantastic — and it's worthless. Find the bug before the reveal. (Hint: ask who computed the scaler's mean and std, and from which rows.)

    from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression

scaler = StandardScaler()
X_scaled = scaler.fit_transform(X)        # something is wrong on THIS line

X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(
    X_scaled, y, test_size=0.25, random_state=0)

model = LogisticRegression().fit(X_train, y_train)
print("test accuracy:", model.score(X_test, y_test))   # looks amazing... and is fake
    Show the bug & the fix

    scaler.fit_transform(X) computes the mean and std over the whole dataset — including the test rows — before the split. The test set's statistics leak into the numbers applied to training. The model has, in a quiet way, already seen the test distribution, so its reported accuracy is optimistic and won't survive real unseen data. This is the exact "data leakage" from Module 2, hiding inside one innocent line.

    The fix — split first, fit the scaler on train only, transform both:

    X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(
    X, y, test_size=0.25, random_state=0)

scaler = StandardScaler()
X_train_s = scaler.fit_transform(X_train)   # learn mu, sigma from TRAIN
X_test_s  = scaler.transform(X_test)        # apply the SAME numbers to test

model = LogisticRegression().fit(X_train_s, y_train)
print("test accuracy:", model.score(X_test_s, y_test))

    Better still: put the scaler in a Pipeline so it is structurally impossible to leak. That's Beat 9.

    09 · the leak-proof way

    ColumnTransformer + Pipeline: leakage becomes impossible.

    Every scikit-learn transform has .fit() (learn the parameters) and .transform() (apply them). fit_transform does both — and belongs on training data only; on test you call transform alone. A ColumnTransformer applies different handling per column; a Pipeline chains steps so they fit together and never leak. Wrap the model inside it and the golden rule enforces itself.

    import pandas as pd
from sklearn.compose import ColumnTransformer
from sklearn.pipeline import Pipeline
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler, OneHotEncoder
from sklearn.impute import SimpleImputer
from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression

# A tiny messy dataset: some numbers, some categories, some blanks.
df = pd.DataFrame({
    "age":   [22, 38, None, 26, 35, 58, 40, None],
    "fare":  [7.25, 71.3, 7.92, 53.1, 8.05, 51.9, 21.0, 13.0],
    "city":  ["NYC", "LA", "NYC", None, "LA", "SF", "SF", "NYC"],
    "survived": [0, 1, 1, 1, 0, 0, 1, 0],
})
X = df.drop(columns="survived")
y = df["survived"]

# Split FIRST, so nothing learns from the test rows (Module 2 leakage rule).
X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(
    X, y, test_size=0.25, random_state=0)

numeric = ["age", "fare"]
categorical = ["city"]

# Numbers: fill blanks with the (training) median, then standardize.
num_pipe = Pipeline([
    ("impute", SimpleImputer(strategy="median")),
    ("scale", StandardScaler()),
])
# Categories: fill blanks with the most frequent value, then one-hot.
# handle_unknown="ignore" makes unseen test categories all-zeros instead of crashing.
cat_pipe = Pipeline([
    ("impute", SimpleImputer(strategy="most_frequent")),
    ("onehot", OneHotEncoder(handle_unknown="ignore")),
])

pre = ColumnTransformer([
    ("num", num_pipe, numeric),
    ("cat", cat_pipe, categorical),
])

model = Pipeline([("pre", pre), ("clf", LogisticRegression())])
model.fit(X_train, y_train)               # fits imputers/scaler/encoder on TRAIN only
print("test accuracy:", model.score(X_test, y_test))

    Because everything lives inside one Pipeline, model.fit(X_train, y_train) learns the medians, the mean/std, and the category list from the training rows alone; model.score(X_test, y_test) applies those frozen numbers to the test rows. There is no way to accidentally leak — that is the entire point of pipelines. (This toy set is tiny; don't read into the exact accuracy. The structure is the lesson.)

    Hands-on: engineer features on messy data (Titanic)

    The Titanic passenger list is the classic playground. Three engineered features reliably help predict survival, each showing a different technique. Predict what Title will hold for "Cumings, Mrs. John" before running.

    import pandas as pd

# Pretend we've loaded the Titanic CSV into a DataFrame `df` with columns like:
# Name, Sex, Age, SibSp (siblings/spouses), Parch (parents/children), Fare.
df = pd.DataFrame({
    "Name": ["Braund, Mr. Owen", "Cumings, Mrs. John",
             "Heikkinen, Miss. Laina", "Allen, Master. William"],
    "SibSp": [1, 1, 0, 0],
    "Parch": [0, 0, 0, 1],
    "Fare":  [7.25, 71.28, 7.92, 21.07],
})

# 1) Title extracted from Name: "Mr", "Mrs", "Miss", "Master" carry age/sex/class signal.
df["Title"] = df["Name"].str.extract(r",\s*([^.]+)\.")
# Group rare titles so we don't one-hot a hundred near-empty columns.
common = ["Mr", "Mrs", "Miss", "Master"]
df["Title"] = df["Title"].where(df["Title"].isin(common), other="Rare")

# 2) Family size: traveling alone vs. in a group changes survival odds.
df["FamilySize"] = df["SibSp"] + df["Parch"] + 1   # +1 counts the passenger
df["IsAlone"] = (df["FamilySize"] == 1).astype(int)

# 3) Fare bins: turn a skewed continuous fare into ordered buckets.
df["FareBin"] = pd.qcut(df["Fare"], q=2, labels=["low", "high"])

print(df[["Title", "FamilySize", "IsAlone", "FareBin"]])

    Four new columns from raw text and counts: Title (a category pulled out with a regular expression, then trimmed so rare titles collapse into "Rare"), FamilySize and IsAlone (interaction-style features combining two raw columns), and FareBin (a continuous number bucketed into ordered groups with pd.qcut, which splits at quantiles so each bin holds roughly equal counts). None existed in the raw data. Each encodes a human hunch about what matters, handed to the model as a ready-made column. That is feature engineering.

    10 · pull it from your own head

    Cold recall — answer before you flip.

    Re-reading feels productive and mostly isn't. Retrieving is. Say each answer out loud first, then flip. Can't? That's your signal to scroll up — but try first.

    Scaling helps gradient descent but not decision trees. Why the difference?
    tap to flip
    GD walks a cost surface that scaling rounds from a skinny ravine into a bowl. A tree splits on thresholds ("x > cut"), and rescaling moves the cut by the same amount — same split either way.
    The z-score formula, and what μ and σ mean?
    tap to flip
    \(z=(x-\mu)/\sigma\). μ = the feature's mean (average). σ = its standard deviation (spread). z = how many std-devs above (+) or below (−) the mean a value sits.
    Why never map {red, blue, green} → {1, 2, 3}?
    tap to flip
    It invents a fake order and spacing (green = 3× red, blue halfway). The model multiplies those by weights. Use one-hot: one yes/no column per category, no false ordering.
    The golden rule of every transform, in five words?
    tap to flip
    Fit on train, transform on both. Learn μ/σ/medians/category-list from training rows only; freeze them; apply to test. Otherwise: leakage.
    What does wrapping everything in a Pipeline buy you?
    tap to flip
    It fits all transforms on train only and applies them to test automatically — leakage becomes structurally impossible, not just a thing you remembered to avoid.
    11 · boss fight
    🤖 Boss fight — with your Claude companion

    A list of "suggested prompts" is lazy. Here is a real challenge loop. Your companion hands you a messy column; you propose features. It pushes back and escalates until you can think like a feature engineer cold.

    You are running a feature-engineering drill with me. Round 1: invent ONE realistic messy column from a tabular dataset (a free-text name, a raw timestamp/date, a high-cardinality category like zip code, a skewed money amount, or a column with blanks). Show me 5 sample values and tell me which model we're training (a tree OR gradient descent). I will reply with: (a) how to clean/transform it, (b) what new feature(s) to engineer from it, (c) the right encoding/scaling, and (d) whether scaling even matters for the model you named. Grade my answer, point out any leakage risk or false-ordering trap I missed, then escalate — make the next column messier (mix a date plus missing values, or a category with thousands of levels). Keep going until I handle two messy columns in a row with no leakage and no false-ordering mistakes. Finish by asking me to explain, in one sentence, why feature engineering and prompt engineering are the same instinct.

    You pass this module when you can take a raw column you've never seen, propose leak-free features for it, say whether scaling matters for the model in play, and explain the prompt-engineering connection — without scrolling back.

    12 · the traps, named

    The mistakes that look fine until they don't.

    • Scaling before the split. The bug you just debugged. fit_transform on the whole set leaks test statistics. Split first; a Pipeline enforces it.
    • Scaling a tree for no reason. Trees and forests (Module 8 — Classical Algorithms) split on thresholds — rescaling changes nothing and just muddies what actually helped.
    • One-hot dummy explosion. A zip-code column becomes thousands of near-empty columns — slow, memory-hungry, overfit-prone. Group rare levels into "Other," or use cross-validated target encoding.
    • Unseen categories at test time. A category that appears only in test crashes a naive encoder. Set handle_unknown="ignore" so it becomes all-zeros.
    • Ordinal-encoding unordered categories. {red, blue, green} → {0,1,2} smuggles in a fake order. One-hot for unordered; ordinal only for true orders.
    • Imputing with the test set's median. Same leak, different transform. Learn the fill value on train, apply to both — what a Pipeline guarantees.
    Two more hand exercises (with solutions)

    1. Standardize [2, 4, 6, 8] by hand. Give μ, σ, and the four z-scores.

    μ = (2+4+6+8)/4 = 20/4 = 5. Squared distances: 9, 1, 1, 9 → sum 20. Variance: 20/4 = 5. σ = √5 ≈ 2.236. Z-scores: (2−5)/2.236 ≈ −1.34, (4−5)/2.236 ≈ −0.45, (6−5)/2.236 ≈ 0.45, (8−5)/2.236 ≈ 1.34. So [−1.34, −0.45, 0.45, 1.34].

    2. Min-max [10, 20, 30], then map the unseen test value 40.

    Train min = 10, max = 30, range = 20. (10−10)/20 = 0, (20−10)/20 = 0.5, (30−10)/20 = 1 → [0, 0.5, 1]. Test 40: (40−10)/20 = 30/20 = 1.5. It lands above 1 — min-max doesn't clip, and unseen values can fall outside [0, 1]. Expected and fine; just don't assume the range holds for new data.

    Module 8

    Classical Algorithms Toolbox

    01 · before any theory

    You already run kNN every day

    New restaurant. You've never eaten there. Is it any good? You don't run a regression. You check the three most similar places you have tried — same neighbourhood, same price, same cuisine — and you guess from those.

    That's it. That's the whole algorithm called k-Nearest Neighbours. You judge a new thing by the things nearest to it. No formula, no training, no theory of the world — just "who are your closest neighbours, and what are they?"

    So here's the only real decision you ever have to make with it. Don't scroll — commit to an answer first.

    You ask k neighbours and take their majority vote. You're deciding between asking 1 neighbour or 500. What's the catch with each extreme?

    Small k = high variance: one outlier next to your query flips the whole prediction. Huge k = high bias: you average in points from across the entire dataset, so every query drifts toward "whatever class is most common overall." The art is the middle. You'll feel this exact trade-off in the lab in about thirty seconds — it's the same bias-variance tug-of-war from Module 6, now something you can drag with a slider.

    02 · go break it yourself

    Lab: drag the query, twist the dial

    Drag the white "?" around the plane. The ringed points are its current nearest neighbours; the shaded regions show what it would predict everywhere. Now slide k from 1 to 15 and watch the boundary breathe. Don't just wiggle it — run the missions. They're built to make one specific thing click.

    LAB k-Nearest Neighbours, live

    Move the query point. Change k. Watch the decision regions redraw.

    • Park the query where it predicts class A at k=1 but flips to class B at k=15 Hint: sit it on top of one lone A that's surrounded by B's. Then explain to yourself WHY it flipped.
    • Set k=1 and trace the boundary — find the jagged, noisy islands Each tiny pocket exists to protect one single training point. That's overfitting you can see.
    • Crank k high and watch the same boundary go smooth and lazy Where did the little islands go? They got out-voted by the crowd.
    Why does the same point flip from A to B?

    At k=1 the prediction is decided by the single closest training point — if that's a stray A, you predict A. At k=15 you poll fifteen points; the lone A is badly outnumbered by all the B's that surround the region, so the vote swings to B. Same query, same data — only the number of voters changed. That one observation is the whole reason k is a hyperparameter you have to tune, not a constant you can ignore.

    Lock this in. Jagged-at-low-k, smooth-at-high-k is the variance-vs-bias picture from Module 6. Every model in this module has its own version of this same knob. Spot it each time.
    03 · now the math, because you earned it

    "Nearest" needs a ruler

    You've been eyeballing "closest." The computer can't eyeball. It needs a number for distance. The default is Euclidean distance — plain straight-line distance, the Pythagorean theorem stretched to as many features as you like.

    \[ d(\mathbf{a},\mathbf{b}) = \sqrt{\sum_{j=1}^{m}(a_j - b_j)^2} \]

    a, b two points, each a list of feature values. m how many features. a_j, b_j feature \(j\) of each point. Plain English: for every feature, subtract, square (kills the sign), sum, square-root.

    To classify a new point: measure its distance to every stored point, keep the \(k\) smallest, let those \(k\) labels vote, most common wins. There is nothing to "fit" — kNN just memorises the data. That's why it's a lazy learner: zero work at training time, all the work at prediction time.

    Your data has age in years (0–100) and salary in dollars (0–200000). You run kNN with no preprocessing. What actually decides "nearest"?

    Distance squares each difference. A salary gap of 10,000 contributes \(10{,}000^2 = 10^8\); the largest possible age gap of 100 contributes only \(10{,}000\). Salary wins by a factor of ten thousand. The age column might as well not exist. Scaling is not optional for kNN — standardize features first (Module 7) or distance is measured almost entirely along whichever column happens to carry the biggest raw numbers.

    04 · you compute it, not me

    The vote, by hand

    Five labelled points, two features each. A new query \( q = (3,3) \). Before you read a single distance: glance at the table and guess the k=3 answer. Then do the arithmetic and see if your gut was right.

    Pointx₁x₂Label
    P112A
    P223A
    P335B
    P454B
    P542B
    Now check your work — all five distances and the vote
    • P1: \( \sqrt{(3-1)^2+(3-2)^2} = \sqrt{4+1} = \sqrt{5} \approx 2.24 \)
    • P2: \( \sqrt{(3-2)^2+(3-3)^2} = \sqrt{1+0} = 1.00 \)
    • P3: \( \sqrt{(3-3)^2+(3-5)^2} = \sqrt{0+4} = 2.00 \)
    • P4: \( \sqrt{(3-5)^2+(3-4)^2} = \sqrt{4+1} = \sqrt{5} \approx 2.24 \)
    • P5: \( \sqrt{(3-4)^2+(3-2)^2} = \sqrt{1+1} = \sqrt{2} \approx 1.41 \)

    Sorted: P2 (1.00, A), P5 (1.41, B), P3 (2.00, B), then P1 and P4 tied at 2.24. The three nearest are P2 (A), P5 (B), P3 (B). Vote: A=1, B=2 → class B. But at k=1 the nearest is P2, so you'd predict A. Same query, different k, different answer — exactly what the lab showed you.

    05 · build it in raw NumPy

    kNN from scratch — predict the print, then run it

    Here's the whole algorithm in pure NumPy. No library magic. You already computed the answer by hand above, so predict every line of output before you run it.

    import numpy as np
from collections import Counter

def knn_predict(X_train, y_train, query, k=3):
    # Euclidean distance from the query to every training point.
    # (X_train - query) broadcasts the query across all rows.
    diffs = X_train - query                      # shape (n_points, n_features)
    dists = np.sqrt(np.sum(diffs**2, axis=1))    # one distance per point
    # Indices of the k smallest distances.
    nearest = np.argsort(dists)[:k]
    nearest_labels = y_train[nearest]
    # Majority vote: most_common returns [(label, count)], take the label.
    winner = Counter(nearest_labels).most_common(1)[0][0]
    return winner, dists, nearest

X_train = np.array([[1,2],[2,3],[3,5],[5,4],[4,2]], dtype=float)
y_train = np.array(["A","A","B","B","B"])
query = np.array([3,3], dtype=float)

pred, dists, nearest = knn_predict(X_train, y_train, query, k=3)
print("distances:", np.round(dists, 2))
print("3 nearest indices:", nearest, "-> labels:", y_train[nearest])
print("prediction:", pred)
    The output

    Distances [2.24 1. 2. 2.24 1.41], three nearest indices [1 4 2] (P2, P5, P3) with labels ['A' 'B' 'B'], and prediction: B — matching your hand calculation exactly. The entire "training" was storing two arrays. All the compute happens at prediction time. That's the lazy-learner tax: cheap to train, expensive to predict.

    06 · the algorithm that asks questions

    Decision trees: 20 questions, automated

    A good 20-questions player never opens with "Is it a platypus?" They ask "Is it alive?" — a question that splits the universe in half. A decision tree is a flowchart of yes/no questions the algorithm builds for itself: at every step it searches every feature and threshold for the single split that best separates the classes, then repeats on each branch. The doctor's instinct — most informative question first — turned into code.

    But "best split" needs a number. How messy is a group of examples? That's Gini impurity.

    \[ G = 1 - \sum_{c=1}^{C} p_c^2 \]

    C number of classes. p_c fraction of the node that is class \(c\). Plain English: square each class's share, add them up, subtract from 1.

    A pure node (all one class) has \(p_c=1\), so \(G = 1-1 = 0\) — spotless. A 50/50 two-class node has \(G = 1-(0.5^2+0.5^2) = 0.5\) — maximum mess. Gini runs from 0 (pure) up to \(1 - 1/C\).

    A node has 9 positives and 1 negative. Without computing exactly — is its Gini closer to 0 or to 0.5?

    \(G = 1-(0.9^2+0.1^2) = 1-(0.81+0.01) = \mathbf{0.18}\). Impurity is about how lopsided the mix is, not whether two classes merely exist. 9-to-1 is very lopsided, so it's far purer than a 4-to-6 node (which sits at 0.48). The closer to all-one-class, the closer to 0.

    To score a candidate split, compute the weighted impurity after the split — each child's Gini, weighted by how many examples land in it:

    \[ G_{\text{split}} = \frac{n_L}{n}G_L + \frac{n_R}{n}G_R \]

    n examples at the parent. n_L, n_R counts going left / right. G_L, G_R the children's Gini. Information gain \(= G_{\text{parent}} - G_{\text{split}}\): how much mess the split cleaned up.

    The tree tries every feature and every threshold and keeps the split with the largest gain. (Some trees use entropy instead of Gini; they behave almost identically — we stick with Gini.)

    Gini of a split, by hand

    Parent: 4 positives, 6 negatives → \(G_{\text{parent}} = 0.48\) (you just saw why). A candidate split sends 4 left (3 pos, 1 neg) and 6 right (1 pos, 5 neg). Work it through:

    • Left: \( G_L = 1 - ((3/4)^2 + (1/4)^2) = 1 - (0.5625 + 0.0625) = 0.375 \)
    • Right: \( G_R = 1 - ((1/6)^2 + (5/6)^2) = 1 - (0.0278 + 0.6944) = 0.2778 \)
    \[ G_{\text{split}} = \tfrac{4}{10}(0.375) + \tfrac{6}{10}(0.2778) = 0.15 + 0.1667 = 0.3167 \]

    Information gain \(= 0.48 - 0.3167 = \mathbf{0.1633}\). The split dropped impurity from 0.48 to ~0.317 — a solid gain. The tree takes it.

    Lock this in. "Fit a tree to the errors" comes back in two screens as boosting — and the \(\eta\) that controls each tree's correction is literally the learning rate from Module 4. Same idea, bigger steps.
    07 · the rest of the cast

    Four more personalities

    Each algorithm below has a completely different theory of the world. Meet them fast, as characters.

    Random forest — "ask a crowd, not one expert"

    A single deep tree is a brilliant but erratic expert: it memorises quirks and overfits. A random forest grows hundreds of trees, each on a bootstrap sample (a same-size dataset drawn with replacement, so rows repeat and some are left out) and each split only allowed to consider a random subset of features (often \(\sqrt{m}\)). No tree is great, but their mistakes are uncorrelated, so the votes cancel the noise. Averaging \(T\) trees shrinks the variance of the random error by roughly a factor of \(T\) while leaving the real signal intact. Training on bootstrap samples like this is called bagging (bootstrap aggregating).

    Gradient boosting — "a team that fixes each other's mistakes"

    Boosting also builds many trees, but sequentially, each one trained to correct what the previous trees got wrong. Start with a constant guess \(F_0\). Each round, fit a small tree \(h_t\) to the current errors and add it in:

    \[ F_t(\mathbf{x}) = F_{t-1}(\mathbf{x}) + \eta\, h_t(\mathbf{x}) \]

    F_t the model after \(t\) rounds. h_t the new little tree, fit to the errors. η (eta) the learning rate — a small number (~0.1) capping each tree's correction. Small \(\eta\) + many trees = careful and accurate.

    This is the gradient-descent idea from Module 4 — except each "step downhill" is taken by adding a whole tree instead of nudging a weight. XGBoost, LightGBM, and CatBoost are the optimized, regularized versions. On tabular data, this is usually the single strongest tool you own.

    Support vector machine — "find the widest safe road"

    An SVM draws a boundary \(\mathbf{w}\cdot\mathbf{x} + b = 0\) — but not just any boundary, the one with the largest possible margin, the widest empty buffer to the nearest points of either class. Wide buffer = confident, robust decision. The points touching the margin are the support vectors; they alone define the boundary, everything else is irrelevant. The kernel trick swaps the dot product \(\mathbf{x}_i\cdot\mathbf{x}_j\) for a kernel \(K(\mathbf{x}_i,\mathbf{x}_j)\) that measures similarity as if the points had been lifted into a much higher-dimensional space — so a straight margin up there shows up as a curved boundary down here, with no high-dimensional coordinates ever computed.

    Naive Bayes — "multiply the clues together"

    Bayes' rule flips a conditional: \(P(\text{class}\mid\text{features}) \propto P(\text{class}) \times P(\text{features}\mid\text{class})\). The "naive" move is pretending the features are independent, so the joint likelihood factors into a clean product:

    \[ P(y\mid x_1,\dots,x_m) \;\propto\; P(y)\prod_{j=1}^{m} P(x_j\mid y) \]

    y the class. x_j feature \(j\). P(y) the class's overall prevalence (prior). P(x_j|y) how likely that feature value is within the class. ∏ multiply over all features. Compute for each class, pick the largest.

    Because it models how the data is generated within each class, naive Bayes is a generative model. Logistic regression (Module 5) models the boundary \(P(\text{class}\mid\text{features})\) directly — it's discriminative. Generative models can train on less data and even invent new samples; discriminative ones usually win on raw accuracy when data is plentiful. Most of deep learning is discriminative — except modern generative AI, which brings the generative idea roaring back at scale (Module 14).

    08 · the real payoff

    Six algorithms, one interface

    The point of this whole module: once you understand each idea, scikit-learn lets you audition the entire toolbox by swapping one line. Six classical models on the same breast-cancer dataset. Note the StandardScaler on kNN and SVM — both are distance-based and need scaled features (you proved why above).

    from sklearn.datasets import load_breast_cancer
from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
from sklearn.pipeline import make_pipeline
from sklearn.neighbors import KNeighborsClassifier
from sklearn.tree import DecisionTreeClassifier
from sklearn.ensemble import RandomForestClassifier, GradientBoostingClassifier
from sklearn.svm import SVC
from sklearn.naive_bayes import GaussianNB

X, y = load_breast_cancer(return_X_y=True)
X_tr, X_te, y_tr, y_te = train_test_split(X, y, test_size=0.25, random_state=0)

models = {
    "kNN (k=5)":        make_pipeline(StandardScaler(), KNeighborsClassifier(n_neighbors=5)),
    "Decision Tree":    DecisionTreeClassifier(max_depth=4, random_state=0),
    "Random Forest":    RandomForestClassifier(n_estimators=200, random_state=0),
    "Gradient Boosting":GradientBoostingClassifier(random_state=0),
    "SVM (RBF)":        make_pipeline(StandardScaler(), SVC(kernel="rbf")),
    "Naive Bayes":      GaussianNB(),
}

for name, model in models.items():
    model.fit(X_tr, y_tr)
    acc = model.score(X_te, y_te)
    print(f"{name:18s} test accuracy = {acc:.3f}")

    Identical API for every model: fit to learn, score to evaluate. All six land in the mid-0.90s, with the tree ensembles and the scaled SVM typically on top and the lone decision tree slightly behind. That uniform fit/predict/score interface is scikit-learn's superpower — a whole toolbox in a for loop.

    Debug it: the kNN entry was sabotaged

    A teammate edited the dict. It runs without errors, but kNN's accuracy quietly cratered into the low 0.80s while every other model stayed fine. The bug is on one line. Find it before you open the fix.

    models = {
    "kNN (k=5)":        KNeighborsClassifier(n_neighbors=5),   # <-- something is wrong on this line
    "Decision Tree":    DecisionTreeClassifier(max_depth=4, random_state=0),
    "Random Forest":    RandomForestClassifier(n_estimators=200, random_state=0),
    "Gradient Boosting":GradientBoostingClassifier(random_state=0),
    "SVM (RBF)":        make_pipeline(StandardScaler(), SVC(kernel="rbf")),
    "Naive Bayes":      GaussianNB(),
}
    Show the bug & why

    The StandardScaler is gone — kNN is fed raw, unscaled features. The breast-cancer columns range from tiny (smoothness ~0.1) to large (area ~2500), so distance is dominated by the few big-magnitude columns and kNN measures "nearest" almost entirely along them. The fix is the exact pipeline you saw above: make_pipeline(StandardScaler(), KNeighborsClassifier(n_neighbors=5)). Same trap as the salary-vs-age predict card — distance-based models must be scaled. (SVM would suffer the same way without its scaler.)

    Production-grade boosting: XGBoost

    Same shape of code, the workhorse of tabular ML:

    # pip install xgboost
from xgboost import XGBClassifier
xgb = XGBClassifier(n_estimators=300, learning_rate=0.1, max_depth=3,
                    subsample=0.8, eval_metric="logloss", random_state=0)
xgb.fit(X_tr, y_tr)
print("XGBoost test accuracy =", round(xgb.score(X_te, y_te), 3))

    The three key knobs: n_estimators (how many trees), learning_rate (how big each correction is — smaller needs more trees), and max_depth (how complex each tree is). These control the same bias-variance trade-off you met in Module 6.

    09 · the decision you'll actually make at work

    Which algorithm when?

    Here's the punchline working data scientists know in their bones: on ordinary tabular data (rows and columns in a spreadsheet), a gradient-boosted tree like XGBoost still routinely beats a neural network. The fancy deep learning of Modules 10–14 rules images and text; this classical toolbox quietly wins the Kaggle competitions and powers the dashboards. A practical decision order for a new spreadsheet problem:

    • Start with a baseline: logistic/linear regression (Modules 3, 5). Fast, interpretable, tells you if the problem is even learnable.
    • Best accuracy with little fuss? Gradient boosting (XGBoost/LightGBM). Handles mixed scales, missing values, and nonlinear interactions; usually wins on tabular data.
    • Robust, low-tuning ensemble? Random forest. Great defaults, hard to break, gives feature importances for free.
    • A model a human can read aloud? A shallow decision tree — every prediction is an explainable chain of questions.
    • Small dataset, clear margins, well-scaled features? SVM can shine, especially with an RBF kernel.
    • Text/spam, very high-dimensional sparse counts, need it fast? Naive Bayes — a strong, cheap baseline.
    • Few features, smooth boundaries, zero training time wanted? kNN — but scale first, and beware large datasets (prediction is slow).

    5 million rows, 80 mixed numeric + categorical features, lots of missing values, you need strong accuracy without much hand-tuning. First reach — and the one to specifically avoid?

    Gradient boosting (XGBoost/LightGBM) eats this for breakfast: mixed feature scales need no scaling, missing values are handled natively, nonlinear interactions are captured automatically, top-tier accuracy with modest tuning. Avoid kNN: with 5 million stored rows it must compute distances to (potentially) all of them at prediction time — painfully slow — plus it degrades in high dimensions and needs careful scaling and categorical encoding first. A single tree would be readable but weaker; naive Bayes is fast but its independence assumption is shaky across 80 correlated features.

    10 · pull it out of your own head

    Cold recall — no scrolling back

    Re-reading feels like learning but mostly isn't. Retrieving is. Answer each in your head first, then flip. If you can't, that's your signal to scroll up — but try first.

    In kNN, what does tiny k vs huge k do to the decision boundary?
    tap to flip
    Tiny k = jagged, noisy boundary that overfits (high variance). Huge k = smooth, lazy boundary that blurs toward the overall majority (high bias). Tune k in between.
    Why must you scale features before kNN or SVM?
    tap to flip
    They measure distance, which squares each feature's difference. A big-unit column (salary) swamps a small-unit one (age) and hijacks "nearest." Standardize first.
    Gini impurity: what value for a pure node? For a 50/50 two-class node?
    tap to flip
    Pure node: G = 0. 50/50 node: G = 1−(0.5²+0.5²) = 0.5, the max for two classes. Lower = cleaner. Trees pick the split with the biggest drop in weighted Gini.
    Bagging vs boosting — what's the difference and what does each reduce?
    tap to flip
    Bagging (random forest): independent trees in parallel, averaged → cuts variance. Boosting (XGBoost): dependent trees in sequence, each fixing the last → cuts bias, but overfits if over-trained.
    Generative vs discriminative — which is naive Bayes, which is logistic regression?
    tap to flip
    Naive Bayes is generative (models how data is generated per class, P(features|class)). Logistic regression is discriminative (models the boundary P(class|features) directly).
    First algorithm for messy tabular data with strong accuracy needs?
    tap to flip
    Gradient boosting (XGBoost/LightGBM). Handles mixed scales, missing values, nonlinear interactions; usually the strongest single tool on tabular data.
    11 · boss fight
    🤖 Boss fight — with your Claude companion

    A box of suggested prompts is lazy. Here's a real challenge loop. Paste it to your Claude Code companion and don't move on until you can name the right algorithm cold and defend it.

    You are drilling me on algorithm selection for the classical ML toolbox: kNN, decision tree, random forest, gradient boosting (XGBoost), SVM, naive Bayes. Describe ONE realistic problem at a time — give the data size, number and type of features (numeric/categorical/text), missing-value situation, interpretability needs, and latency constraints. Ask me which algorithm I'd reach for first and which I'd specifically avoid, and why. After I answer by hand, grade me: was my pick defensible, did I name the real reason (e.g. kNN's predict-time cost, scaling needs, boosting's missing-value handling, naive Bayes' independence assumption), and did I catch the trap? Then escalate — make the next scenario ambiguous, with a tempting wrong answer. Mix in one or two where I must also compute a Gini impurity or a kNN vote by hand. Keep going until I get three scenarios in a row fully right.

    You pass this module when you can, for any described problem, name a sensible first algorithm AND the one to avoid — and explain both reasons in plain words without peeking at the guide.

    12 · extra reps (optional)

    Three more by hand

    1. Classify \( q=(3,3) \) from the table with k=1 and with k=5. What does each predict?
      Show solution
      Sorted distances: P2 (1.00, A), P5 (1.41, B), P3 (2.00, B), P1 (2.24, A), P4 (2.24, B). k=1 → nearest is P2 → A. k=5 → all points: A, B, B, A, B → A=2, B=3 → B. Tiny k is jumpy; larger k follows the overall majority.
    2. Gini of a node with 9 positives and 1 negative — purer or messier than the 4-pos/6-neg node (0.48)?
      Show solution
      \(G = 1-(0.9^2+0.1^2) = 1-(0.81+0.01) = 0.18\). Much purer than 0.48 — most examples agree, far less uncertainty.
    3. A split sends 5 left (5 pos, 0 neg) and 5 right (0 pos, 5 neg) from a parent of 5 pos / 5 neg. Parent Gini, weighted split Gini, information gain?
      Show solution
      Parent \(G = 1-(0.5^2+0.5^2) = 0.5\). Both children pure → \(G_L = G_R = 0\). \(G_{\text{split}} = \tfrac{5}{10}(0)+\tfrac{5}{10}(0) = 0\). Gain \(= 0.5 - 0 = 0.5\) — a perfect split, the maximum possible gain for a 50/50 parent.
    Where this returns. The bias-variance knob (k here) shows up in every model you'll ever tune. Boosting's \(\eta\) is the learning rate from Module 4. The generative idea, niche here, comes roaring back at enormous scale in Module 14's generative AI. And the fit/predict/score interface you looped over is the same one you'll use for neural networks. One toolbox, many returns.
    Module 9

    Unsupervised Learning

    01 · No labels, no teacher

    Here is a pile of dots. Nobody told you what they are.

    Every model you've built so far had a teacher. Module 3 had house prices. Module 5 had spam/not-spam. Each training row came with the right answer stapled to it, and the model just learned to copy the mapping. That's supervised learning.

    Now I'm taking the answer column away. I hand you 150 points scattered on a table — no colors, no labels, no key — and I ask: are there groups in here? How many? Where? You have no teacher. Just the raw positions.

    This is the situation for 99% of the data anyone actually has. Nobody annotated it. So before we name a single algorithm, sit with the real question: can a machine find structure with nobody telling it the answer? Look at a scatter of dots and decide, in your head, how many groups you see and roughly where their centers are. Commit to a number. Then we'll see if a machine agrees.

    You have unlabeled customer data and want to "find the natural groups." A teammate says "let's just train a classifier." What's wrong with that?

    A classifier is supervised — it learns a mapping from inputs to known answers. No answers, no classifier. When the answer column is missing, you need a method that invents the structure itself. That's unsupervised learning, and clustering is its flagship move.

    Three questions show up over and over when you have unlabeled data, and each has a classic tool. Keep these straight — they're the map for the whole module:

    which rows group together? → clustering (we use k-means).   which rows are abnormal? → anomaly detection.   can I describe each row with fewer numbers? → dimensionality reduction (we use PCA) → embeddings.

    02 · The trick is dumber than you think

    K-means is two steps, on repeat, forever.

    Here's the whole algorithm before any math. Drop k flags on the table at random — call them centroids. Then loop two steps until nothing moves:

    1. Assign. Every point joins the nearest flag's team. Closest centroid wins.

    2. Update. Each flag walks to the center — the average position — of its current team.

    That's it. After the flags move, some points near a boundary suddenly have a different nearest flag, so they switch teams. Re-average. Re-assign. Each round everything settles a bit more, until a round changes nothing. That last part has a name: convergence.

    The party version, because it makes the loop physical: three friends — Ana, Ben, Cleo — stand in random corners of a room. Every guest walks to the nearest host (assign). Each host then shuffles to the middle of their little crowd (update). A host moving means a few guests near the edge realize someone else is now closer, so they switch crowds. Hosts re-center. After a few rounds nobody wants to move. Final host positions are the centroids; the crowds are the clusters. K-means is this dance done with arithmetic instead of feet.

    A centroid currently sits to the LEFT of its team's true middle. You run one update step. Where does it move?

    Update sets each centroid to the mean of its members — full stop. If it's sitting left of the crowd, the mean is to its right, so it hops right. It doesn't take a cautious half-step; it teleports straight to the average. (No learning rate here — unlike Module 4's gradient descent, k-means moves all the way each time.) You're about to watch this exact move in the lab.

    03 · The lab

    Run the dance yourself. Then break it on purpose.

    Now do it, don't watch it. Step runs one phase at a time — first points recolor to their nearest centroid (✕), then each centroid glides to the mean of its members. Run loops until a round changes nothing. Reset / new centroids reseeds the starting flags. Don't just poke at it — finish the three missions. Each one teaches a different way k-means can surprise you.

    LAB K-means, with missions

    Predict each outcome before you click. The gap between your guess and what happens is the part that sticks.

    • Count the iterations to convergence Set the right k, then hit Step (assign → update) over and over until a round changes nothing. How many full rounds did it take? On clean blobs it's usually fewer than you'd guess — often under 10.
    • Set k WRONG and watch it shred a real cluster Pick a k bigger than the true number of groups. Run to convergence. Notice how k-means happily slices one genuine blob in half — it will always return exactly k clusters, real or not.
    • Reseed until you get a BAD answer Keep the right k, but hit Reset / new centroids and run, several times. Eventually two flags start inside the same blob and converge to a lopsided, wrong split. That's initialization sensitivity, live.
    What you should have seen

    Mission 1: convergence is fast and obvious — the centroids stop twitching and the colors freeze. Mission 2: too-big k carves a clean blob into pieces because the algorithm has no concept of "this group is already done"; it just minimizes distance with the budget of centroids you gave it. Mission 3: most reseeds find the good answer, but some don't — the same algorithm on the same data lands in a worse arrangement purely because of where the flags started. That's not a bug. It's why real k-means restarts several times and keeps the best run.

    04 · Earn the notation

    You already invented it. Here's the math.

    You have \( n \) data points \( x_1, x_2, \dots, x_n \) (each \( x_i \) is a point, maybe with several coordinates). You pick \( k \), the number of clusters. K-means keeps \( k \) centroids \( \mu_1, \dots, \mu_k \) (the symbol \( \mu \), "mu", is just a name for a centroid — a point in the same space). Every data point gets a label \( c_i \) saying which cluster it's in. Your two-step dance becomes two formulas.

    \[ c_i = \arg\min_{j \in \{1,\dots,k\}} \; \lVert x_i - \mu_j \rVert^2 \]

    assign step. For point \( i \), try every centroid \( \mu_j \), measure how far the point is, keep the index \( j \) of the closest. ‖x − μ‖² is the squared distance: subtract coordinate by coordinate, square, add up. arg min means "the argument \( j \) that makes this smallest."

    \[ \mu_j = \frac{1}{|S_j|} \sum_{i \in S_j} x_i \]

    update step. Each centroid becomes the mean of its members. S_j is the set of points currently labeled \( j \). |S_j| is how many there are. Add up the points in cluster \( j \), divide by the count — the ordinary average, per coordinate.

    And here's the quantity k-means is secretly minimizing the whole time — the within-cluster sum of squares, also called inertia:

    \[ J = \sum_{i=1}^{n} \lVert x_i - \mu_{c_i} \rVert^2 \]

    inertia J. For every point, take the squared distance to its own centroid \( \mu_{c_i} \), and sum over all points. Lower \( J \) means tighter, more compact clusters.

    Here's the elegant bit: the assign step lowers \( J \) (each point picks its closest centroid), and the update step lowers \( J \) too (the mean is the single point that minimizes summed squared distance to a group). Both steps only ever decrease \( J \), so the algorithm is guaranteed to stop — though possibly at a not-quite-best arrangement. That's exactly the bad answer you reseeded into in Mission 3.

    05 · Do it by hand

    One full iteration, just you and arithmetic.

    Six points on a line (1D, so each point is just a number): 1, 2, 4, 7, 8, 12. We pick \( k = 2 \) and start with centroids \( \mu_1 = 2 \) and \( \mu_2 = 8 \). In 1D the distance is just \( |x - \mu| \). Before you read the worked solution, do the assign step yourself — for each point, which centroid is closer?

    After assigning all six points to the nearer of \( \mu_1 = 2 \) and \( \mu_2 = 8 \), which split do you get?

    Point 4 is distance 2 from \( \mu_1=2 \) but distance 4 from \( \mu_2=8 \) → cluster 1. Point 7 is distance 5 from \( \mu_1 \) but only 1 from \( \mu_2 \) → cluster 2. So {1, 2, 4} and {7, 8, 12}.

    BY HAND the full iteration

    Step 1 — Assign. For each point, compare distance to each centroid and pick the smaller:

    • Point 1: \( |1-2| = 1 \) vs \( |1-8| = 7 \). Nearer 2 → cluster 1.
    • Point 2: \( |2-2| = 0 \) vs \( |2-8| = 6 \) → cluster 1.
    • Point 4: \( |4-2| = 2 \) vs \( |4-8| = 4 \) → cluster 1.
    • Point 7: \( |7-2| = 5 \) vs \( |7-8| = 1 \) → cluster 2.
    • Point 8: \( |8-2| = 6 \) vs \( |8-8| = 0 \) → cluster 2.
    • Point 12: \( |12-2| = 10 \) vs \( |12-8| = 4 \) → cluster 2.

    So cluster 1 = {1, 2, 4} and cluster 2 = {7, 8, 12}.

    Step 2 — Update. Move each centroid to the mean of its members:

    \[ \mu_1 = \frac{1 + 2 + 4}{3} = \frac{7}{3} \approx 2.33, \qquad \mu_2 = \frac{7 + 8 + 12}{3} = \frac{27}{3} = 9 \]

    New centroids: \( \mu_1 \approx 2.33 \) and \( \mu_2 = 9 \). That's one complete iteration.

    What happens on round two? Re-assign with the new centroids: 1, 2, 4 are still nearest 2.33; 7, 8, 12 are still nearest 9. No point switches, so the means don't move. Converged after a single update. The final inertia: \( (1-2.33)^2 + (2-2.33)^2 + (4-2.33)^2 + (7-9)^2 + (8-9)^2 + (12-9)^2 \approx 1.78 + 0.11 + 2.78 + 4 + 1 + 9 = 18.67 \).

    06 · Find the bug

    This from-scratch k-means looks right. One line is wrong.

    Below is k-means in plain NumPy. It runs without errors — but the clusters come out nonsensical and the reported inertia is garbage. The update logic is correct, the stop logic is correct. Read it, find the broken line before you open the spoiler.

    import numpy as np

def kmeans(X, k, n_iters=100, seed=0):
    rng = np.random.default_rng(seed)
    # 1) Initialize: pick k distinct data points as starting centroids.
    centroids = X[rng.choice(len(X), size=k, replace=False)]

    for _ in range(n_iters):
        # 2) ASSIGN: distance from every point to every centroid.
        dists = np.linalg.norm(X[:, None, :] - centroids[None, :, :], axis=2)
        labels = dists.argmax(axis=1)            # <-- something is wrong on this line

        # 3) UPDATE: each centroid becomes the mean of its assigned points.
        new_centroids = np.array([
            X[labels == j].mean(axis=0) if np.any(labels == j) else centroids[j]
            for j in range(k)
        ])

        # 4) STOP if nothing moved (converged).
        if np.allclose(new_centroids, centroids):
            break
        centroids = new_centroids

    inertia = ((X - centroids[labels]) ** 2).sum()  # within-cluster sum of squares
    return labels, centroids, inertia
    Show the bug & why

    labels = dists.argmax(axis=1) assigns every point to its farthest centroid. The assign step is supposed to pick the nearest one, so it must be argmin, not argmax:

    labels = dists.argmin(axis=1)

    One letter. With argmax, points flee to the opposite side of the room every round, the centroids chase phantom crowds, and inertia (which still measures distance to your own centroid) stays huge. This is the unsupervised cousin of Module 4's flipped sign — a tiny direction error that quietly breaks the whole loop while the code runs perfectly happily.

    Here's the corrected version, with the broadcasting trick spelled out. Predict the output first: three blobs centered at (0,0), (4,5), (5,0) — what should the recovered centroids and inertia look like?

    import numpy as np

def kmeans(X, k, n_iters=100, seed=0):
    rng = np.random.default_rng(seed)
    # 1) Initialize: pick k distinct data points as starting centroids.
    centroids = X[rng.choice(len(X), size=k, replace=False)]

    for _ in range(n_iters):
        # 2) ASSIGN: squared distance from every point to every centroid.
        # X[:, None, :] has shape (n, 1, d); centroids[None] has (1, k, d);
        # broadcasting gives an (n, k) table of distances.
        dists = np.linalg.norm(X[:, None, :] - centroids[None, :, :], axis=2)
        labels = dists.argmin(axis=1)            # nearest centroid per point

        # 3) UPDATE: each centroid becomes the mean of its assigned points.
        new_centroids = np.array([
            X[labels == j].mean(axis=0) if np.any(labels == j) else centroids[j]
            for j in range(k)
        ])

        # 4) STOP if nothing moved (converged).
        if np.allclose(new_centroids, centroids):
            break
        centroids = new_centroids

    inertia = ((X - centroids[labels]) ** 2).sum()  # within-cluster sum of squares
    return labels, centroids, inertia

# Make three round blobs centred at (0,0), (4,5), (5,0).
rng = np.random.default_rng(42)
X = np.vstack([
    rng.normal([0, 0], 0.6, size=(50, 2)),
    rng.normal([4, 5], 0.6, size=(50, 2)),
    rng.normal([5, 0], 0.6, size=(50, 2)),
])

labels, centroids, inertia = kmeans(X, k=3, seed=1)
print("centroids:\n", np.round(centroids, 2))
print("inertia:", round(inertia, 1))
print("cluster sizes:", np.bincount(labels))

    This prints centroids hugging the three true centers — [[-0.02 -0.04], [4.01 4.98], [4.98 -0.06]] — an inertia of about 93.0, and cluster sizes: [50 50 50]: a perfect recovery. Now change seed=1 to seed=0 and you get a worse inertia of roughly 707 with sizes like [24 26 100] — two starting centroids landed in the same blob and never escaped. That one-character change is the "initialization matters" pitfall from Mission 3, live in code.

    07 · The library way + choosing k

    scikit-learn does the same thing, but smarter.

    scikit-learn runs the same algorithm — but with k-means++ initialization and several restarts to dodge bad starts (so you stop losing to the seed lottery), tested and fast. It exposes inertia_ so you can build an elbow plot, and ships PCA for dimensionality reduction. Standardize before PCA, and usually before k-means too. Predict the elbow output before you read on: the data is genuinely 3 blobs — at which k should inertia stop dropping sharply?

    import numpy as np
from sklearn.cluster import KMeans
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
from sklearn.decomposition import PCA

rng = np.random.default_rng(42)
X = np.vstack([
    rng.normal([0, 0], 0.6, size=(50, 2)),
    rng.normal([4, 5], 0.6, size=(50, 2)),
    rng.normal([5, 0], 0.6, size=(50, 2)),
])

# --- Clustering with k-means ---
km = KMeans(n_clusters=3, n_init=10, random_state=0)
labels = km.fit_predict(X)
print("inertia_:", round(km.inertia_, 1))          # ~93.0
print("centroids:\n", np.round(km.cluster_centers_, 2))

# --- Elbow method: inertia for k = 1..6 ---
for k in range(1, 7):
    model = KMeans(n_clusters=k, n_init=10, random_state=0).fit(X)
    print(f"k={k}  inertia={model.inertia_:.0f}")

# --- PCA (standardize first!) ---
Xs = StandardScaler().fit_transform(X)
pca = PCA(n_components=2).fit(Xs)
print("explained_variance_ratio_:", np.round(pca.explained_variance_ratio_, 3))

    The k-means part reports the same inertia_ of about 93.0 we got from scratch — reassuring. The elbow loop prints inertias of roughly 1641, 720, 93, 80, 69, 59 for k = 1 to 6: a huge drop down to k = 3, then a near-flat tail. That sudden bend is the elbow — the textbook signal that k = 3, exactly the number of blobs we built. The PCA part prints explained_variance_ratio_: [0.657 0.343]: the first principal component captures about 66% of the spread, and the two together capture 100% (this data is genuinely 2D, so nothing is lost).

    why the elbow, not the minimum? Inertia always falls as k rises — with k equal to the number of points, every point is its own centroid and \( J = 0 \). So "pick the k with lowest inertia" is a trap that screams "use one cluster per row." Plot \( J \) vs \( k \) and find the bend: before it, each new cluster buys a big drop; after it, you're just splitting already-tight groups for nothing.

    08 · The other two questions

    Anomalies and PCA, in plain words.

    Anomaly detection — learn "normal," flag the rest. Sometimes you don't want groups, you want the one weird row. The method: build a picture of what "normal" looks like from your mountain of ordinary data, then flag whatever doesn't fit. Why unsupervised? With fraud, machine faults, or network intrusions you have tons of normal examples and almost no examples of the rare bad thing — and tomorrow's fraud won't look like yesterday's. A supervised classifier (Module 5) needs many labeled examples of both classes. Anomaly detection only needs to know what normal looks like, so it can catch new kinds of weird it has never seen.

    PCA — fewer numbers, same story. A dataset with 50 columns lives in 50 dimensions: impossible to picture, slow to compute on, and usually redundant (height in cm and height in inches carry the same information twice). PCA asks: along which direction do the points spread out the most? That direction — the first principal component — captures the most "story." Project every point onto it and you get one number per point, chosen so those numbers are as spread out as possible, because spread = information. The second component is the best direction perpendicular to the first, and so on. Keep the first two or three and you can plot 50-dimensional data on a flat page while losing very little.

    scikit-learn reports explained_variance_ratio_: the fraction of total variance each component captures. If the first two ratios add to 0.95, two numbers preserve 95% of the spread of the whole dataset. The intuition — "find the directions of maximum variance" — is all you need; the underlying machinery (eigenvectors of the covariance matrix) is a Module 1 deep-dive.

    You run PCA on raw data where one column is salary (range 0–200,000) and another is age (range 0–100), without standardizing. What dominates the first principal component?

    PCA chases variance, and variance is measured in the raw units. A column ranging over 200,000 swamps one ranging over 100 for no real reason — it's an artifact of scale, not meaning. Standardize first (subtract the mean, divide by the standard deviation, as in Module 7) so each feature gets a fair say. Same scaling trap bites k-means too, since it judges everything by distance.

    09 · Where this goes next

    From PCA to embeddings — the bridge to the rest of the course.

    PCA hands you a few numbers per row by finding directions of maximum variance — a computed low-dimensional representation. An embedding is the same idea taken further: a low-dimensional vector that a model learns so that "similar things sit close together." A word embedding might place king, queen, and monarch near each other in, say, 300 numbers; a song embedding places similar songs nearby; a face embedding places photos of the same person nearby. A streaming service stores each listener as a short embedding, then clusters those — clustering on embeddings is a standard combo. The difference from PCA: embeddings are learned from a task (predict the next word, tell faces apart), so they capture meaning, not just spread.

    Lock this in. The "similar things sit close together in a low-dimensional space" idea you just met in PCA is the seed of embeddings, which you'll build for real in Module 13 (Sequences, NLP, and the Road to Attention) and see power Module 14 (Transformers and LLMs). And the k-means trap "feature scale distorts distance" is the exact fairness issue from Module 7 — standardize, every time.
    10 · The traps that actually bite

    Read these once now; you'll hit every one.

    None of these are exotic. They are the mistakes everyone makes on their first real clustering job.

    • "K-means works on any cluster shape." It assumes round, roughly equal-sized blobs, because it judges everything by distance to a single center. It mangles long thin shapes, rings, and clusters of very different sizes. For those, reach for DBSCAN or Gaussian mixtures.
    • Forgetting to scale. Raw distance means a feature in the thousands (salary) drowns one in the tens (age). Standardize first unless features already share units.
    • Trusting a single run. Random init can land two centroids in one blob (you saw seed=0 do exactly this — inertia 707 vs 93). Always use n_init > 1 and keep the lowest-inertia result.
    • Picking k by minimum inertia. Inertia hits zero when k equals the number of points, so "lowest inertia" says "one cluster per row." Use the elbow, a silhouette score, and domain knowledge.
    • Treating PCA components as features. A component is a blend of all your columns (e.g. "0.3 × height + 0.5 × weight − 0.2 × age"). It maximizes variance, not interpretability — don't expect "PC1 = income."
    • Confusing clusters with truth. K-means always returns k clusters, even on data with none. The groups are a hypothesis to investigate, not a fact.
    11 · Cold recall — no scrolling back

    Pull it out of your own head.

    Re-reading feels like learning but mostly isn't. Retrieving is. Answer each one in your head first, then flip. Can't? That's your signal to scroll up — but try first.

    What are the two repeating steps of k-means, and which quantity does each lower?
    tap to flip
    Assign each point to its nearest centroid, then update each centroid to the mean of its members. Both steps only ever lower inertia (within-cluster sum of squares), so it always converges.
    Why can't you choose k by picking the lowest inertia?
    tap to flip
    Inertia always falls as k rises and hits 0 when k = number of points. "Lowest inertia" means "one cluster per row." Use the elbow instead — the k after which extra clusters barely help.
    Same data, same k, two different answers. What's the cause and the fix?
    tap to flip
    Initialization sensitivity. Where the centroids start changes where they converge. Fix: k-means++ init plus several restarts (n_init > 1), keep the lowest-inertia run.
    One sentence: what does PCA do, and why standardize first?
    tap to flip
    PCA finds the directions of maximum variance to describe each row with fewer numbers. Standardize first so a column doesn't dominate just because its raw units are bigger.
    When does anomaly detection beat a supervised classifier?
    tap to flip
    When you have lots of "normal" data, very few labeled bad examples, and the bad thing keeps changing (fraud, faults, intrusions). Model normal, flag deviations — catches novel weirdness a classifier never saw.
    12 · Boss fight
    🤖 Boss fight — with your Claude companion

    A list of suggestions is lazy. Here's a real challenge loop. Paste it to your Claude Code companion and don't move on until you can run a full k-means iteration in your head, cold.

    You are drilling me on k-means until I have it cold. Give me 5 or 6 points in 1D, a value of k=2, and two starting centroids. I will do ONE full iteration BY HAND — the assign step (which point goes to which centroid) and the update step (the new centroid positions) — and tell you my answer plus whether it has converged. Grade each part, show exactly where I slipped if I did, then escalate: move to 2D points, then ask me to predict whether a given initialization will converge to a good or bad clustering and why, then quiz me on why increasing k always lowers inertia and why that breaks "minimize inertia." Keep going until I get two rounds in a row perfect.

    You pass when you can run an assign-then-update iteration by hand, explain which step lowers inertia and why both must, and say out loud why a too-big k or a bad seed wrecks the result — using the party picture, not a memorized formula.

    Stretch exercises

    1. One k-means iteration by hand. Points (1D): 0, 1, 6, 10, 11. Start with centroids \( \mu_1 = 1 \) and \( \mu_2 = 10 \). Do one assign step and one update step. What are the new centroids?
      Show solution

      Assign: 0 → |0−1|=1 vs |0−10|=10 → cluster 1. 1 → 0 vs 9 → cluster 1. 6 → |6−1|=5 vs |6−10|=4 → cluster 2. 10 → cluster 2. 11 → cluster 2. So cluster 1 = {0, 1}, cluster 2 = {6, 10, 11}. Update: \( \mu_1 = (0+1)/2 = 0.5 \); \( \mu_2 = (6+10+11)/3 = 27/3 = 9 \). New centroids: 0.5 and 9.

    2. You cluster customers using two raw features: annual spend (range 500–50,000) and number of visits (range 1–40). Without scaling, which feature dominates, and what do you do?
      Show solution

      Spend ranges over tens of thousands while visits range over tens, so a difference of "1000 in spend" swamps "10 in visits." Distance — and therefore the clusters — are driven almost entirely by spend; visits barely matter. Fix it by standardizing both features (subtract the mean, divide by the standard deviation) so each contributes on a comparable scale before running k-means.

    3. For fraud detection you have 2,000,000 normal transactions and only 30 known fraud cases, and fraudsters keep inventing new tricks. Supervised classifier or anomaly detection?
      Show solution

      Anomaly detection is the stronger start. A classifier needs many examples of both classes; with 30 fraud cases it has almost nothing to learn "fraud" from, and it would predict "normal" for everything to score high accuracy (the imbalance trap from Module 6). It's also blind to genuinely new fraud styles. Anomaly detection models the abundant normal behavior and flags deviations, so it can catch novel fraud it has never seen. Once you accumulate enough confirmed fraud labels, add a supervised model (or a hybrid).

    4. PCA on a standardized 10-feature dataset gives explained_variance_ratio_ = [0.52, 0.23, 0.11, 0.06, 0.04, 0.02, 0.01, 0.005, 0.003, 0.002]. How many components keep at least 90% of the variance, and what does that tell you?
      Show solution

      Add until you cross 0.90: 0.52, then 0.75 (two), then 0.86 (three), then 0.92 (four). So 4 components keep about 92% — just under with 3 (86%), comfortably over with 4. The 10 original features are highly redundant: four learned directions carry almost all the spread, so you can compress to 4 numbers per row (great for plotting in pairs, speeding up later models, or de-noising) while losing under 10% of the information.

    You beat this module when you can run an assign-then-update iteration by hand, explain why increasing k always lowers inertia (and why that makes the elbow necessary), decide between clustering / anomaly detection / a supervised model for a given problem, and say in one sentence what PCA does, why you must standardize first, and how an embedding differs from a PCA projection.
    Module 10

    Neural Network Fundamentals

    01 · the line that can't

    Draw one line. Win the puzzle. (You can't.)

    Two light switches, A and B. The light comes on when exactly one switch is up. Both up: dark. Both down: dark. One up, one down: bright. That's XOR — exclusive or.

    Here are the four cases on a grid. The two ● are "light on", the two ○ are "light off":

    B=1   ●       ○
    B=0   ○       ●
            A=0   A=1

    ● = light ON (one switch up). ○ = light OFF (both same). Your one job: separate the ● from the ○ with a single straight line.

    Grab a pen. Genuinely try. Put both ● on one side of a straight line and both ○ on the other. Go ahead — I'll wait.

    You can't. The ● sit on one diagonal, the ○ on the other. They're interlocked, like the black and white squares of a checkerboard. No straight cut separates them. Every model you've built so far — including the logistic regression from Module 5 — draws exactly one straight line. So all of them top out at 50% on XOR: a coin flip. This is the wall. The rest of this module is how we smash it.

    02 · what's actually in the box

    A neuron is embarrassingly simple

    Before we stack anything, look at the unit we're stacking. You already built one in Module 5 and didn't know it had a fancy name. A neuron takes some inputs, multiplies each by a weight, adds them up, adds one more number called the bias, then runs the total through a squashing function. That's the whole machine. Multiply, add, squash.

    A neuron computes w1*x1 + w2*x2 + b, then squashes. Stack TWO of these layers with NO squashing in between — just raw weighted sums feeding into more weighted sums. What can the resulting two-layer thing represent?

    A weighted sum of weighted sums is still a weighted sum. Algebraically, two stacked linear layers multiply out into one weight matrix and one bias — a single straight line, no matter how many you stack. We'll prove it with real numbers in a moment. The squashing function (the activation) is the only thing that stops the collapse. Without it, a 100-layer network is just a fancy line. That nonlinearity is the entire reason neural nets work.

    03 · the math you just invented

    One neuron, named precisely

    You described it in words; here's the notation. A neuron with inputs \( x_1, x_2, \dots, x_m \) computes two things. First the pre-activation (the raw linear score), called \( z \):

    \[ z = w_1 x_1 + w_2 x_2 + \dots + w_m x_m + b \]

    \( x_j \) the j-th input number. \( w_j \) the weight on it — how strongly that input matters (we tune these). \( b \) the bias — shifts the score up or down regardless of inputs (also tuned). \( z \) the weighted sum plus bias.

    Then the neuron applies an activation function \( g \) to get its output, the activation \( a \):

    \[ a = g(z) \]

    g a fixed, no-tuning function whose only job is to bend the straight line into a curve. a the neuron's final output number.

    That's it. Multiply, add, squash. The weights and bias are the knobs training turns; \( g \) is hardwired. Now do one by hand before any code runs.

    DO Compute a neuron by hand

    Inputs \( \mathbf{x} = [1, 2] \), weights \( \mathbf{w} = [0.5, -1] \), bias \( b = 1 \), sigmoid activation. Work out \( z \), then \( a = \sigma(z) \). Use \( e \approx 2.718 \). Write your answer before you peek.

    Show the worked answer

    \( z = (1)(0.5) + (2)(-1) + 1 = 0.5 - 2 + 1 = -0.5 \).

    Squash: \( a = \sigma(-0.5) = \dfrac{1}{1 + e^{0.5}} = \dfrac{1}{1 + 1.6487} = \dfrac{1}{2.6487} \approx 0.3775 \).

    So on this input the neuron outputs about 0.3775 — a touch below 0.5, leaning toward "no". That is the entire computation a neuron performs. Every neuron in every network you'll ever build does exactly this.

    The sigmoid, your friend from Module 5, is the squashing function we just used:

    \[ \sigma(z) = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-z}} \]

    e ≈ 2.718, Euler's number. \( \sigma(z) \) squashes any score into the range \( (0, 1) \) — reads perfectly as a probability. Its slope has a famously tidy form: \( \sigma'(z) = \sigma(z)\,(1-\sigma(z)) = a(1-a) \). If you already have the activation \( a \), the slope is just \( a(1-a) \) — no touching \( e \) again. Backprop loves this.

    04 · lab — meet the squashers

    Where does the gradient vanish?

    Two activations you must know. Sigmoid bends gently and flattens at both ends. ReLU (Rectified Linear Unit) is brutally simple — max(0, z): output \( z \) if positive, otherwise 0. Don't take my word for the shapes. Go look, and turn on the derivative.

    LAB Activation functions + their slopes

    Switch between Sigmoid, Tanh, ReLU, Leaky ReLU. Tick "show derivative" to overlay each function's slope. The derivative is the gradient that backprop multiplies through — so where the derivative goes to zero, learning dies.

    • Select Sigmoid, show its derivative, and find its tallest point The peak slope is only 0.25, and it sits at z = 0.
    • Drag z out to +6 and -6 on sigmoid — watch the derivative It collapses to nearly 0 at both ends. This is the vanishing gradient: a saturated neuron barely responds.
    • Switch to ReLU and read its derivative A clean step: 0 for negatives, 1 for positives. It never shrinks toward zero on the positive side.

    You stack 10 sigmoid layers. Backprop multiplies the slope of each layer together as it walks back. Each sigmoid slope is at most 0.25. What happens to the gradient reaching the FIRST layer?

    \( 0.25^{10} \approx 10^{-6} \). The early layers receive a gradient so tiny they barely learn — the vanishing gradient problem. That's why ReLU dominates hidden layers in modern deep nets: its slope is a clean 1 on the positive side, so it doesn't choke the signal. For our shallow 2-2-1 network either activation is fine, but you'll diagnose and fix this properly in Module 11.

    05 · lab — wire them together

    Now stack neurons into a network

    A neural network is nothing more exotic than many neurons wired together: the outputs of one batch of neurons become the inputs of the next. Picture a "2-2-1" network — 2 inputs, 2 hidden neurons, 1 output:

    Every arrow is a weight (a number to learn). Each green and orange circle also has a bias and an activation. Information flows left to right — that's the forward pass. The two hidden neurons each carve their own straight cut; the output neuron combines them into a bent, kinked boundary. That combination is exactly what XOR needs: not one line, but two lines joined.

    The committee of advisors. A CEO must make a yes/no call but only listens to two advisors. Advisor A fires up whenever "A on, B off"; advisor B fires up whenever "B on, A off".
