So you'll see 63 errors out of 200 (31.5% error rate), blurry largest at 40 (= 20.0% of all data) and occluded second at 23 (= 11.5%). The ordering and the "share of all data" column hand you the same verdict as the by-hand sheet — now reproducible on thousands of examples.

    06 · find the bug

    This evaluation looks fine. It's lying to you.

    A beginner wrote this to "check if the model is good." It runs clean, prints a great number, and the model is worthless. The bug isn't a typo or a crash — it's a thinking error. Find it before you open the answer.

    from sklearn.tree import DecisionTreeClassifier

# fraud detection: 0.8% of transactions are fraud (label 1)
model = DecisionTreeClassifier()
model.fit(X_train, y_train)

preds = model.predict(X_test)
accuracy = (preds == y_test).mean()      # <-- the wrong question entirely
print(f"Accuracy: {accuracy:.1%}")       # prints 99.2%  -> "ship it!"

# (no baseline computed, no precision, no recall)
    Show the bug & why

    Two linked sins, and neither is a syntax error. (1) Wrong metric. Fraud is 0.8% of transactions, so a model that predicts "never fraud" for everything scores \(1 - 0.008 = 99.2\%\) accuracy — identical to this one. The 99.2% measures nothing. (2) No baseline. Nobody computed "always predict the majority class," so there's no way to see that the fancy tree didn't beat the dumbest possible guess. The fix: report precision and recall (or PR-AUC) against an explicit majority-class baseline, because the real costs are missing fraud (false negative) and annoying honest customers (false positive) — and accuracy is blind to both on imbalanced data. Pick the metric that matches the cost of each error before you train.

    07 · ship it

    Save the model, then put it behind a link

    Once a model is good enough you need two things: save & reload it (so you don't retrain every time) and let other people use it. For scikit-learn, joblib saves; for PyTorch, torch.save stores the weights. Then Gradio turns any Python function into a web page in a few lines.

    import joblib
from sklearn.datasets import load_iris
from sklearn.ensemble import RandomForestClassifier

# 1) Train a quick model (you'd use your real, evaluated model here).
data = load_iris()
clf = RandomForestClassifier(n_estimators=100, random_state=0)
clf.fit(data.data, data.target)

# 2) SAVE it to disk so you never have to retrain to use it.
joblib.dump(clf, "iris_model.joblib")

# 3) LOAD it back (e.g. in a separate app file or after a restart).
clf = joblib.load("iris_model.joblib")
print(clf.predict([[5.1, 3.5, 1.4, 0.2]]))   # -> array([0])  (setosa)

    Trains an iris classifier, writes it to iris_model.joblib, loads it back, predicts on one flower — printing [0], the "setosa" class. This split — save in one program, load in another — is exactly what lets your training script and your serving app be two separate things.

    Now wrap the loaded model in a web UI. gr.Interface takes a function, a list of input widgets, and an output widget, and builds the whole page — boxes, a Submit button, a result area — automatically.

    import gradio as gr
import joblib

clf = joblib.load("iris_model.joblib")
names = ["setosa", "versicolor", "virginica"]

def classify(sepal_len, sepal_wid, petal_len, petal_wid):
    features = [[sepal_len, sepal_wid, petal_len, petal_wid]]
    probs = clf.predict_proba(features)[0]        # one prob per class
    return {names[i]: float(probs[i]) for i in range(3)}

demo = gr.Interface(
    fn=classify,
    inputs=[
        gr.Number(label="sepal length (cm)", value=5.1),
        gr.Number(label="sepal width (cm)",  value=3.5),
        gr.Number(label="petal length (cm)", value=1.4),
        gr.Number(label="petal width (cm)",  value=0.2),
    ],
    outputs=gr.Label(num_top_classes=3),
    title="Iris classifier",
    description="Enter four measurements; see the predicted species.",
)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    demo.launch()   # local web page; share=True gives a public link

    Run with python app.py and Gradio prints a local URL (like http://127.0.0.1:7860). Open it: four number boxes, a Submit button, a bar chart of class probabilities — built from a function and a list. Pass share=True to launch() for a temporary public link you can text to a friend. This is the fastest path from "a model on my laptop" to "something a human can actually try."

    LAB from notebook to public link

    No widget here — your laptop is the lab. Take any model you trained in an earlier module and ship it. Tick these as you go.

    • Save a trained model to disk, restart Python, load it back, and predict joblib.dump / joblib.load, or torch.save / load_state_dict
    • Wrap it in a Gradio Interface and open the local URL match the input widgets to your real features
    • Get a public link and send it to one person who isn't you launch(share=True)
    Gradio for humans, FastAPI for software. Gradio is perfect for demos and for letting non-programmers poke at your model. But when another program needs predictions — a mobile app, a website backend, a scheduled job — you want plain JSON, not a web UI. That's FastAPI: a function decorated with @app.post("/predict") that takes JSON in and returns JSON out, serving many requests efficiently. Rule of thumb: Gradio for humans to click, FastAPI for software to call. Plenty of teams ship both.
    08 · the part that gets people hurt

    Average accuracy is a liar

    A model that's accurate on average can be systematically wrong for one group of people — and that's how ML causes real harm. A loan model with 92% overall accuracy might be 95% for one group and 80% for another. The average hides the gap. The fix is a discipline, not a button: check your metrics separately for each subgroup, not just overall.

    Three concrete fairness questions you can ask of almost any classifier, given a sensitive attribute (gender, race, age group) you must not discriminate on:

    • Demographic parity: does the model approve each group at similar rates? Compare \( P(\hat{y}=1 \mid \text{group A}) \) with \( P(\hat{y}=1 \mid \text{group B}) \), where \( \hat{y}=1 \) means "approved." Big gaps deserve scrutiny.
    • Equal opportunity: among people who actually deserve approval, does the model approve each group at similar rates? Compares the true positive rate across groups — often the fairer question: of the qualified people, are we treating groups equally?
    • Calibration: when the model says "70% likely," is that true for every group? If "70%" means 70% for one group but 50% for another, the scores mean different things for different people.

    These can conflict — you usually can't satisfy all three at once, which is itself the point: fairness is explicit trade-offs and value choices, not a single button. Here's a minimal subgroup audit you can run on any predictions.

    import numpy as np

def subgroup_audit(y_true, y_pred, group):
    """Print accuracy and positive-prediction rate per group."""
    y_true, y_pred, group = map(np.array, (y_true, y_pred, group))
    print(f"{'group':<10}{'n':>6}{'accuracy':>11}{'approval rate':>16}")
    for g in np.unique(group):
        m = group == g
        acc = (y_pred[m] == y_true[m]).mean()
        approval = (y_pred[m] == 1).mean()       # rate of positive class
        print(f"{str(g):<10}{m.sum():>6}{acc:>11.1%}{approval:>16.1%}")

# Toy data: group B is approved far less often than group A.
y_true = [1,1,0,1, 1,0,0,0]
y_pred = [1,1,0,1, 0,0,0,0]
group  = ["A","A","A","A", "B","B","B","B"]
subgroup_audit(y_true, y_pred, group)

    Group A is approved 75% of the time, group B 0% — a glaring disparity you'd never see from overall accuracy alone (which is 87.5%). Running this breakdown before you ship is the single most practical fairness habit. Real audits use libraries like fairlearn, but the idea is exactly this loop: slice by group, compare the metrics, investigate gaps.

    Harm is concrete, not abstract. Documented ML failures: résumé screeners that downranked women; healthcare risk scores that gave Black patients lower priority for the same illness (the model used past spending as a proxy for need, and less had historically been spent on them); facial recognition with far higher error rates on darker-skinned faces; recidivism scores that flagged Black defendants high-risk more often. The common thread every time: the data encoded historical inequity, no one audited subgroups, and the system shipped anyway. Auditing isn't optional polish — it's part of building the thing correctly.
    09 · cold recall

    Pull it out of your own head

    Re-reading feels like learning and mostly isn't. Retrieving is. Answer each in your head, then flip. If you can't, that's your signal to scroll up — but try first.

    Your model is 90% accurate. What's the very first move?
    tap to flip
    Open the mistakes and read them. Error analysis before any retraining — it tells you which fix is even worth doing.
    Train error 2%, val error 13%, human 1%. Bias or variance? Fix?
    tap to flip
    Variance (gap of 11%) dominates. Overfitting. Cure: more data, regularization, or a simpler model. A bigger model makes it worse.
    What is "potential gain" for an error bucket?
    tap to flip
    errors in that category ÷ total examples evaluated. It caps the most accuracy you can ever recover by fixing that one cause.
    99.2% accuracy on fraud (0.8% positive). Good?
    tap to flip
    No. "Never fraud" scores 99.2% too — that's the baseline this didn't beat. Report precision/recall, not accuracy, on imbalanced data.
    Gradio or FastAPI?
    tap to flip
    Gradio = a web UI for humans to click. FastAPI = a JSON endpoint for other software to call. Many teams ship both.
    Why can a 92%-accurate model still be unfair?
    tap to flip
    The average hides subgroups. Slice metrics by group; a model can be 95% for one group and 80% for another.
    10 · the boss fight

    Stop following. Start building.

    Tutorial hell is the comfortable trap of always following someone else's steps. You feel productive, but you're building their project, not the skill of starting from a blank file. The cure is uncomfortable on purpose: pick a question you care about, find or scrape your own messy data, and walk this module's loop with no tutorial holding your hand. Getting stuck and unsticking yourself is the learning — and it's exactly where a Claude Code companion shines as a pair-programmer instead of a lecturer.

    🤖 Boss fight — with your Claude companion

    This is the final boss of the whole course: an independent project, mentored not narrated. Don't paste a tutorial. Paste this, then drive.

    Be my ML project mentor, not a tutorial. I want to ship one real project end-to-end and I do the work. Step 1: ask me what question I actually care about, then help me pick a concrete prediction target and ONE primary metric, and make me write down the "good enough to ship" bar before any code. Step 2: have me find or scrape my own messy data and build a dumb baseline FIRST; do not let me train a fancy model until I report the baseline number. Step 3: after I train something, make ME paste 10-15 of its misclassified examples, then quiz me to bucket them by cause and compute potential gain before you suggest a fix. Step 4: given my human/train/val errors, ask me whether it's bias or variance and what to do, and correct me if I'm wrong. Step 5: walk me through saving the model and wrapping it in a Gradio app with a public link. Step 6: force a subgroup fairness audit and make me justify any gap. At each step: ask one sharp question, wait for my answer, grade it, and only advance when I've actually done the thing. If I try to skip a step or reach for a bigger model to fix variance, call it out. Keep going until I have a shipped link plus a README covering my error analysis and fairness check.

    You pass this module — and honestly, you pass the course — when you have a public link to something you built end-to-end: a baseline you beat on purpose, an error-analysis paragraph, and a subgroup audit you can defend. Not a tutorial you finished. A thing that exists because of you.

    If you want a target, in rough order of difficulty:

    • Predict something local: house prices in your city, or whether a tweet on a topic is positive — find data, build a baseline, beat it, deploy a Gradio demo.
    • Reproduce then twist: take a Kaggle competition, get a baseline submission in, then change one thing and measure whether it helped.
    • Fine-tune a small model: a pretrained model from Hugging Face on your own small labeled dataset (ties back to Module 14).
    • Ship it end-to-end: data → model → saved artifact → Gradio app with a public link → a README with your error analysis and fairness check. This is the one to put in front of an employer.
    11 · where to go after this

    Five things worth your next year

    You're past beginner now. These are the resources that actually compound, not a link dump:

    • An Introduction to Statistical Learning (ISLP) — the gold-standard free textbook for the statistics under everything you've learned. Read it cover to cover next.
    • fast.ai — Practical Deep Learning — top-down, project-first; built to get you shipping real things and out of tutorial hell.
    • Andrew Ng — ML & Deep Learning Specializations — the "Structuring ML Projects" course is the definitive deep dive on the bias/variance and error-analysis decisions you did by hand here.
    • Karpathy — Neural Networks: Zero to Hero — build a neural net and a GPT from scratch; the best way to truly own Modules 10–14.
    • Hugging Face LLM Course — hands-on with modern transformers and deployment; pairs naturally with the Gradio skills above. (And the Gradio Quickstart for the 5-minute version.)

    Train on these before you go

    1. A fraud detector achieves 99.2% accuracy; fraud is 0.8% of transactions. Is it good? What metric should you report, and what baseline also scores ~99%?
      Show solution

      Almost certainly useless. "Never fraud" scores \(1 - 0.008 = 99.2\%\) too — that's the baseline this model hasn't beaten. Accuracy is the wrong metric for such imbalance. Report precision and recall (or PR-AUC), because the real costs are missing fraud (false negative) and annoying honest customers (false positive). You'd want high recall on fraud while keeping false positives tolerable. See Modules 5 and 6.

    2. Human error 1%, training error 2%, validation error 13%. Compute avoidable bias and variance, say which dominates, name two fixes and one that would not help.
      Show solution

      Avoidable bias \(= 2\% - 1\% = 1\%\) (small). Variance \(= 13\% - 2\% = 11\%\) (large). Variance dominates — overfitting. Fixes: more data (most reliable), regularization or a simpler model, or data augmentation for images. What would not help: a bigger model — more capacity overfits even more. Bias is already nearly handled at 1%.

    3. Error analysis on 100 examples, 25 errors: 12 "cluttered background," 8 "object too small," 5 "actually mislabeled." Compute max gain per bucket. What first, and why?
      Show solution

      Potential gain = errors ÷ total. Cluttered \(12/100 = 12\%\). Small object \(8/100 = 8\%\). Mislabeled \(5/100 = 5\%\). Do mislabeled first despite being smallest — those 5 aren't model errors, so true accuracy is already \((75+5)/100 = 80\%\), and 5% noisy labels corrupt both training and evaluation. Then attack cluttered background (largest gain, 12 pts) via augmentation or more cluttered training images. Small object is third.

    4. A loan model has 91% overall accuracy. Audit: under-25 approved 30% of the time at 78% accuracy; over-25 approved 65% at 94% accuracy. Name two possible causes and two concrete next steps before shipping.
      Show solution

      Causes: (1) Historical bias / imbalance — if the bank rarely approved young applicants, the model learned and perpetuates that, regardless of true creditworthiness. (2) Fewer/noisier examples for under-25, so the model is genuinely worse for them (note 78% vs 94%). (3) A feature acting as an age proxy (e.g. "years of credit history"). Next steps: (a) measure a fairness criterion explicitly — e.g. equal opportunity, comparing true-positive rate across age groups among applicants who actually repaid; (b) collect/up-weight more young-applicant data and audit which features drive the gap, removing age proxies; (c) involve domain/legal stakeholders — lending fairness is regulated and the "right" trade-off is a value decision. Do not ship until the gaps are understood and justified.

    The whole journey, in one line. Look back. Linear regression (Module 4) found a line by nudging weights: w ← w − α·gradient. Logistic regression (Module 5) reused that exact line. Neural nets (Module 10) used it too — backprop is just a clever way to compute the gradient that same loop consumes. Adam (Module 11) is that line with a smarter step size. The transformer behind every LLM (Module 14)? Still w ← w − α·gradient, run a few trillion times. Every model in this course learned the same way; the only things that changed were the shape of the model and how you fed it data — and this module taught you the part that decides whether all of it actually helps a single human being. You started unable to define a model. You can now frame a problem, train one, diagnose it, deploy it, and audit it for harm. That's the full arc of a working practitioner. The last step is the one no course can take for you: finish a project of your own. Pick something you genuinely want to exist, point your skills and your companion at it, and walk the loop until it ships. You don't need permission and you don't need to feel "ready." You already are. Go build it.
    Project Track

    Project Track — Five Quests

    00 · the only test that matters

    Close every tab. Open a blank file. Now build a spam filter.

    Did your stomach drop? Good. That feeling — blank file, no tutorial to copy, no "next" button — is the exact gap between watching ML and doing ML. Everything you've studied so far has been training wheels. This is the part where you ride.

    Here's the brutal truth about tutorials: they feel like learning because the code runs and the dopamine hits. But you were following turn-by-turn GPS, not building a map in your head. Take the GPS away and you're lost in a parking lot you "drove through" ten times. That trap has a name — tutorial hell — and the only escape is to start from a goal and a blank file, get stuck, and claw your way out. The struggle is the learning. A tutorial hands you the answer; a project forces you to invent the question.

    So this track isn't five lessons. It's five quests, ramping in difficulty, each one a self-contained build you could put in front of a hiring manager. Beat all five and you've shipped: an honestly-evaluated classifier, a model bake-off on real data, a deployed image recognizer, a pretrained-language-model app, and a question-answering bot over your own documents. That is a real ML portfolio — not a certificate, an artifact trail.

    You watch a 6-hour "build an ML project" video, coding along the whole time, every cell green. The next day you open a blank file to do it solo. Most honest prediction?

    Recognition is not recall. Following along lights up the "yeah, that makes sense" circuit and leaves the "produce it from nothing" circuit untouched — and only the second one shows up at a blank file or a job. The fix is uncomfortable on purpose: do these quests without a complete solution in front of you. Get stuck. Debug it yourself first. Aim to rebuild each quest from scratch the second time. If you can do that, it's yours.

    01 · your party member

    Bring your Claude companion — but make it coach, not carry.

    You're not soloing these. Your Claude Code companion lives in your terminal, reads your files, runs your code, and reasons about your errors. The temptation is to let it just do the quest. Resist that — a carried player learns nothing. Use it like a patient senior engineer who refuses to write your code but will happily explain why yours is on fire.

    The difference between leveling up and staying stuck is in how you ask. Watch:

    You hit a traceback you don't understand. Which message to your companion actually makes you better?

    A and B get you a working file and a brain that learned nothing — you'll hit the same bug next week. C makes you close the loop, which is the only part that sticks. Rule of thumb: if you couldn't re-explain a line your companion wrote, you haven't learned it — ask it to slow down and teach that line.

    Four moves to keep in your pocket for every quest:

    • Scaffold, don't solve: "Give me a commented skeleton — function names and TODOs only, no implementations — for a train/validate/test pipeline. I'll write the bodies."
    • Explain, don't fix: "Here's my traceback and code. Explain the cause, give me a hint, let me write the fix."
    • Review like a mentor: "Review this notebook for data leakage, an unfair split, or misleading metrics. List issues with severity; don't rewrite it."
    • Quiz me cold: "Ask me 5 questions about why I made the modeling choices in this file, and push back if my answers are hand-wavy."
    02 · gear up

    Set up once, then never think about it again.

    Everything here runs free in Google Colab (no install, free GPU for the deep-learning quests) or locally. Local needs just a few libraries. Quests 3–5 are far faster with a GPU, so use Colab for those if you don't have one — don't burn an afternoon training a CNN on a laptop CPU out of stubbornness.

    # One-time local setup (skip entirely if you use Google Colab — preinstalled there).
# In a terminal:
#   python -m venv ml-env && source ml-env/bin/activate   # Windows: ml-env\Scripts\activate
#   pip install numpy pandas scikit-learn matplotlib
#   pip install torch torchvision            # Quest 3+
#   pip install transformers datasets gradio # Quests 3-5
#   pip install sentence-transformers faiss-cpu  # Quest 5
    03 · Quest 1 of 5 · after Module 6

    Quest 1 — The Honest Classifier

    A model that reports 96% accuracy and a model you can trust are two different things. This quest is about the second one. Anyone can call .fit(); your job is to evaluate so honestly that you'd stake your name on the number.

    The goal. Train a classifier on small tabular data and evaluate it the right way: a proper train / validation / test split and cross-validation, reported with a confusion matrix and per-class precision/recall — not a lonely accuracy number. Reuses the splitting, CV, and confusion-matrix ideas from Module 6 — Model Evaluation.

    The data. Start with Iris (3 flower species, 150 rows, 4 features) via sklearn.datasets.load_iris — tiny and clean, perfect for getting the pipeline right. Then run the exact same pipeline on the messier Kaggle Titanic dataset (predict who survived), which forces you to handle missing values and categorical columns. Doing both teaches the real lesson: the process stays the same even when the data gets ugly.

    LAB Quest 1 milestones

    Check each off as you complete it in your own notebook. No skipping milestone 2 — the entire quest hinges on it.

    • Load and eyeball the data shapes, class balance, a few rows. Never model data you haven't looked at.
    • Split into train / validation / test hold out the test set first (~20%) and do NOT touch it again until the very end.
    • Train a simple baseline logistic regression or a decision tree, on the training set only.
    • k-fold cross-validate on the training data estimate performance without peeking at test, and compare two model choices fairly.
    • Final evaluation, exactly once retrain on all training data, then score on the untouched test set a single time.
    • Confusion matrix + classification report then write 3 sentences: which classes get confused, and would accuracy alone have hidden it?

    How you know you won. You can state your test accuracy and per-class precision/recall; your test set was never used for any decision before the final score; your CV result is reported as mean ± spread (e.g. 0.96 ± 0.03); and you can point at the confusion matrix and name exactly which mistakes your model makes.

    Stretch. Port the pipeline to Titanic with preprocessing (impute missing ages, one-hot encode categoricals — a preview of Module 7). Plot a decision boundary on two Iris features. Pit a decision tree against logistic regression with CV and decide which you'd deploy.

    Starter code — a correct skeleton for the Iris half. Predict the printed output before you run it, then port it to Titanic yourself.

    import numpy as np
from sklearn.datasets import load_iris
from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split, cross_val_score
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.metrics import confusion_matrix, classification_report

# 1) Load and inspect
data = load_iris()
X, y = data.data, data.target
print("Shape:", X.shape, "| Classes:", np.bincount(y), "| Names:", list(data.target_names))

# 2) Hold out a test set we will NOT touch until the end.
#    stratify=y keeps the class proportions the same in both splits.
X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(
    X, y, test_size=0.20, random_state=42, stratify=y)

# 3) Cross-validate on the TRAINING data to estimate performance honestly.
model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)
cv_scores = cross_val_score(model, X_train, y_train, cv=5)
print(f"5-fold CV accuracy: {cv_scores.mean():.3f} +/- {cv_scores.std():.3f}")

# 4) Train on all training data, then evaluate ONCE on the untouched test set.
model.fit(X_train, y_train)
y_pred = model.predict(X_test)

print("\nConfusion matrix (rows = true, cols = predicted):")
print(confusion_matrix(y_test, y_pred))
print("\n", classification_report(y_test, y_pred, target_names=data.target_names))

# YOUR TURN: write 3 sentences interpreting the confusion matrix.
# Which two species get confused? Would plain accuracy have hidden that?

    Expected: 5-fold CV accuracy around 0.95–0.97, and a near-perfect test confusion matrix whose only mistakes are between versicolor and virginica — the two species that genuinely overlap. That's the whole lesson in one observation: your model's errors aren't random, and the confusion matrix shows you where in a way a single accuracy number never can.

    🤖 Boss fight — defend your evaluation

    Anyone can run the cells. The boss is whether you can defend why the pipeline is honest. Paste this to your Claude companion and don't move on until it can't poke a hole in you.

    You are a skeptical ML reviewer grilling me on my Iris/Titanic classifier. Ask me one pointed question at a time about evaluation honesty — pick from: why the test set stays untouched until the end, what data leakage is and how my split avoids it, why I report cross-validation mean AND spread, why stratify matters, and why accuracy alone could mislead on imbalanced classes. After each answer, grade it (correct / partial / wrong), tell me exactly what was missing, then ask a harder follow-up. If I'm hand-wavy, push back and make me be precise. Keep going until I get three in a row clean.

    You pass Quest 1 when you can explain, unprompted, why touching the test set early quietly inflates your reported number.

    04 · Quest 2 of 5 · after Module 8

    Quest 2 — The Model Bake-Off

    Beginners ask "which algorithm is best?" Engineers ask "best on this data, by this metric, measured fairly?" This quest makes you run the bake-off and discover the quiet workhorses of real-world tabular ML: random forests and gradient boosting.

    The goal. On a regression task, build a fair head-to-head between (a) a linear model, (b) a random forest, and (c) gradient boosting — with sensible feature engineering — and decide which wins and why. Uses Module 7 — Feature Engineering and Module 8 — Classical Algorithms.

    The data. California Housing (predict median house value from 8 features) via sklearn.datasets.fetch_california_housing. It's ideal because the features — median income, house age, location — reward thoughtful engineering. For a classification version, reuse Titanic from Quest 1 or grab any Kaggle tabular set you like.

    LAB Quest 2 milestones

    The trap in this quest is leakage. Milestone 2 is what keeps the comparison honest — do it before you touch a model.

    • Explore the data distributions, correlations, missing values, obvious outliers. Note which features look predictive.
    • Build a preprocessing Pipeline scaling, imputation, encoding via sklearn.pipeline.Pipeline, fit on training data only — this is what prevents leakage.
    • Engineer at least two new features e.g. rooms-per-household or an interaction term, then check whether they actually help.
    • Cross-validate all three families fairly same splits, same metric — RMSE for regression, F1 or ROC-AUC for classification.
    • Tune the strongest model a small grid or randomized search on its key hyperparameters.
    • Results table + feature importances + conclusion which model, which features mattered, and the accuracy vs. interpretability vs. speed tradeoff.

    How you know you won. One clean table compares all three models on the same cross-validated metric; preprocessing lives inside a pipeline so nothing leaks from validation into training; your tuned best model beats the linear baseline on the test set; and you can name the top 3 features driving predictions and explain why they make sense.

    Stretch. Add XGBoost or LightGBM and see if they edge out scikit-learn's gradient boosting. Plot predicted vs. actual and find where the model is most wrong. Use permutation importance instead of built-in importances and discuss the difference. Add a learning curve to diagnose whether more data would help (callback to Module 6).

    DEBUG One line leaks the test set into training. Find it before you peek.

    A teammate's "fair" bake-off reports a suspiciously great score. It runs clean, no errors — and it's cheating. Read it. The bug isn't a typo; it's a workflow error.

    from sklearn.datasets import fetch_california_housing
from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split, cross_val_score
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression

X, y = fetch_california_housing(return_X_y=True)

# "Standardize everything up front so all models see clean features."
scaler = StandardScaler()
X = scaler.fit_transform(X)                 # &lt;-- something is wrong on this line

X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(
    X, y, test_size=0.2, random_state=0)

model = LinearRegression()
print(cross_val_score(model, X_train, y_train, cv=5, scoring="neg_root_mean_squared_error"))
    Show the bug & why

    scaler.fit_transform(X) is fit on the entire dataset — including the test rows — before the split. The scaler's mean and standard deviation are computed using information from data you're pretending you haven't seen yet. That's data leakage: your test score is now optimistically biased, and the whole bake-off is rigged. Fix: put scaling inside a Pipeline (or fit the scaler on X_train only and merely transform the test set). With a pipeline, cross-validation re-fits the scaler on each fold's training portion automatically — which is exactly milestone 2. Leakage is the single most common way a beginner's "amazing" model quietly lies.

    🤖 Boss fight — justify your winner

    A results table is not a decision. The boss is whether you can defend your pick out loud. Paste this and grind it until your reasoning holds.

    You are a hiring manager reviewing my California Housing model bake-off. Interrogate me one question at a time: why I chose RMSE, how my pipeline prevents leakage, why a random forest or boosting might beat a linear model here, what feature importances tell me and how they could mislead, and whether my tuned model's gain over baseline is real or noise. Grade each answer, name what's missing, escalate. If I justify my winner only by "it had the best number," push hard on cost/benefit — interpretability, training speed, robustness. Keep going until I can defend my choice without you finding a hole.

    You pass Quest 2 when you can explain why the tree-based models often crush a linear baseline on messy tabular data — and when they wouldn't.

    05 · Quest 3 of 5 · after Module 12

    Quest 3 — The Deployed Image Classifier

    First you'll train a convolutional net by hand. Then you'll get a far stronger one almost for free — and the gap between those two is the most important practical idea in deep learning. Then you'll ship it as a web app a stranger can use.

    The goal. Train a CNN to classify images, then beat your from-scratch CNN using a pretrained model (transfer learning), and deploy the result as an interactive Gradio web app where someone uploads or draws an image and sees the prediction. Uses Modules 10–12 (neural nets, training, CNNs) plus deployment from Module 15.

    The data. Start with MNIST handwritten digits (28×28 grayscale, 10 classes) via torchvision.datasets.MNIST — small, fast, trains in minutes even on CPU. Then level up to Oxford-IIIT Pets (37 cat/dog breeds, real color photos), where from-scratch training struggles and transfer learning shines — making its value impossible to miss.

    You have ~3,700 pet photos (37 breeds, 100 each). You train a CNN from scratch vs. fine-tune a ResNet-18 pretrained on millions of generic internet images. What happens?

    3,700 photos is nowhere near enough to learn good visual features from zero — the from-scratch net overfits or flounders. The pretrained backbone already learned the universal low-level vocabulary of vision (edges, fur texture, ear shapes) from millions of images; you just teach a fresh final layer to map those features to your 37 breeds. That's transfer learning, and on small datasets it's not a small win — it's the difference between "barely works" and "ships." You'll feel this firsthand when MNIST-from-scratch is fine but Pets-from-scratch isn't.

    LAB Quest 3 milestones

    Milestone 1 (visualize a batch) is non-optional — half of all deep-learning bugs are silently wrong labels or normalization you'd have caught by looking.

    • Load data with DataLoaders and visualize a batch so you trust your labels and normalization before training a thing.
    • Train a small CNN from scratch a couple of conv layers + pooling + a linear head. Track train and validation accuracy per epoch.
    • Swap in a pretrained model e.g. ResNet-18 from torchvision.models; freeze the backbone, train only a new classification head.
    • Compare the two head-to-head accuracy, training time, epochs each needed.
    • Save the better weights, write a single predict(image) function this is the bridge from notebook to app.
    • Wrap predict in a Gradio interface and launch it then upload/draw your own images and watch it work.

    How you know you won. Your from-scratch CNN exceeds ~98% on MNIST test; on Pets, transfer learning clearly beats from-scratch with far less training; you can explain in plain words why a model pretrained on millions of images transfers to your 37 breeds; and the Gradio app correctly classifies an image you provide yourself.

    Stretch. Add data augmentation (random flips/crops) and measure the gain. Unfreeze the top layers and fine-tune at a low learning rate. Show softmax confidence in the app. Deploy publicly with share=True or to Hugging Face Spaces and send a friend the link.

    Starter code — a correct from-scratch CNN on MNIST plus a Gradio app. Predict the per-epoch accuracy trend before running, then add the transfer-learning model yourself.

    import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
from torch.utils.data import DataLoader
from torchvision import datasets, transforms

device = "cuda" if torch.cuda.is_available() else "cpu"

# 1) Data: normalize to MNIST's known mean/std.
tfm = transforms.Compose([transforms.ToTensor(),
                          transforms.Normalize((0.1307,), (0.3081,))])
train_ds = datasets.MNIST(".", train=True,  download=True, transform=tfm)
test_ds  = datasets.MNIST(".", train=False, download=True, transform=tfm)
train_dl = DataLoader(train_ds, batch_size=128, shuffle=True)
test_dl  = DataLoader(test_ds,  batch_size=256)

# 2) A small CNN from scratch.
class SmallCNN(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self):
        super().__init__()
        self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(1, 16, 3, padding=1)   # 1 -> 16 channels
        self.conv2 = nn.Conv2d(16, 32, 3, padding=1)  # 16 -> 32 channels
        self.fc1 = nn.Linear(32 * 7 * 7, 64)
        self.fc2 = nn.Linear(64, 10)                  # 10 digit classes

    def forward(self, x):
        x = F.max_pool2d(F.relu(self.conv1(x)), 2)    # 28 -> 14
        x = F.max_pool2d(F.relu(self.conv2(x)), 2)    # 14 -> 7
        x = x.flatten(1)
        x = F.relu(self.fc1(x))
        return self.fc2(x)                            # raw logits

model = SmallCNN().to(device)
opt = torch.optim.Adam(model.parameters(), lr=1e-3)
loss_fn = nn.CrossEntropyLoss()

# 3) Train for a few epochs.
for epoch in range(3):
    model.train()
    for xb, yb in train_dl:
        xb, yb = xb.to(device), yb.to(device)
        opt.zero_grad()
        loss = loss_fn(model(xb), yb)
        loss.backward()
        opt.step()

    # Evaluate on the test set.
    model.eval()
    correct = total = 0
    with torch.no_grad():
        for xb, yb in test_dl:
            xb, yb = xb.to(device), yb.to(device)
            preds = model(xb).argmax(1)
            correct += (preds == yb).sum().item()
            total += yb.size(0)
    print(f"epoch {epoch}: test accuracy = {correct / total:.4f}")

torch.save(model.state_dict(), "mnist_cnn.pt")

# 4) Deploy with Gradio. (pip install gradio)
import gradio as gr
import numpy as np
from PIL import Image

def predict(img):
    # In current Gradio, a Sketchpad returns a dict; the drawing is under
    # "composite" (an RGBA HxWx4 array). Older versions returned a plain array,
    # so handle both. We then convert to a single 28x28 grayscale digit.
    if img is None:
        return {}
    if isinstance(img, dict):
        img = img.get("composite")
        if img is None:
            return {}
    arr = np.asarray(img)
    # MNIST is white digits on a black background. The sketchpad strokes are
    # opaque, so the alpha channel (where you drew) already gives that polarity.
    if arr.ndim == 3 and arr.shape[2] == 4:
        gray = arr[:, :, 3]                          # alpha channel = where you drew
    else:
        gray = np.asarray(Image.fromarray(arr.astype(np.uint8)).convert("L"))
    pil = Image.fromarray(gray.astype(np.uint8)).resize((28, 28))
    x = torch.tensor(np.array(pil), dtype=torch.float32).reshape(1, 1, 28, 28)
    x = (x / 255.0 - 0.1307) / 0.3081                # same normalization as training
    model.eval()
    with torch.no_grad():
        probs = F.softmax(model(x.to(device)), dim=1)[0]
    return {str(i): float(probs[i]) for i in range(10)}

demo = gr.Interface(fn=predict,
                    inputs=gr.Sketchpad(image_mode="RGBA"),
                    outputs=gr.Label(num_top_classes=3),
                    title="Draw a digit")
demo.launch()  # add share=True for a public link

# YOUR TURN: add a ResNet-18 (torchvision.models.resnet18) transfer-learning
# version on Oxford-IIIT Pets and compare accuracy + training time.

    After 3 epochs the from-scratch CNN should hit roughly 98–99% test accuracy on MNIST, and the Gradio window lets you draw a digit and watch the top-3 probabilities update live. The lesson lands when you redo this on Pets: from-scratch flounders, while a frozen pretrained backbone with a fresh head reaches strong accuracy in a fraction of the time.

    The goal.     07 · Quest 5 of 5 · the capstone · after Module 14

    Quest 5 — RAG Over Your Own Docs

    This is the architecture behind most real LLM products shipping today — and you're going to build it over your own documents. Ask it a question, and it answers from your files instead of making things up. Beat this and you've built something people actually pay for.

    The goal. A small Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) Q&A app: index your documents into a vector store, retrieve the most relevant passages for a question, and have a language model answer using only those passages — deployed via Gradio. Uses everything from Module 14 plus the embedding intuition from Modules 9 / 13.

    The data. Use your own documents — a few PDFs, lecture notes, your notes from this course, a product manual, anything text. Real personal data makes this unforgettable. Embeddings via sentence-transformers, vector search via FAISS (faiss-cpu).

    Your docs never use the word "energy." You ask "How do cells make energy?" Your RAG retriever — searching by embedding similarity, not keywords — returns…

    Embeddings map text to vectors where meaning lives close together, regardless of exact wording. "How do cells make energy?" lands near "the mitochondrion produces ATP" even though they share no words — because the model learned they're about the same thing. That semantic matching is why RAG beats keyword search, and why it can ground an LLM in your specific documents instead of its fuzzy training memory.

    LAB Quest 5 milestones

    The capstone has five stages. Milestone 6 — testing that it refuses to answer what it doesn't know — is what separates a real RAG system from a confident liar.

    • Chunk your docs split into overlapping windows of a few hundred words each, so every chunk is a self-contained passage.
    • Embed every chunk and store in FAISS one vector per chunk, indexed for fast nearest-neighbor search.
    • Retrieve top-k for a question embed the question the same way, pull the most similar chunks.
    • Build the grounded prompt paste retrieved chunks as context, instruct the LLM to answer USING ONLY that context — and to say "I don't know" if it's not there.
    • Wrap it in a Gradio chat interface question in, grounded answer out.
    • Test the refusal ask things that ARE and ARE NOT in your docs; verify it won't hallucinate when it shouldn't know.

    How you know you won. A question whose answer is in your docs returns a correct answer plus the source passage it used; a question outside your docs makes it say it doesn't know rather than inventing an answer; and you can explain each stage — chunk, embed, index, retrieve, generate — in one sentence apiece.

    Stretch. Show the retrieved source chunks alongside each answer (citations). Compare chunk sizes and top-k values and watch the quality tradeoff. Swap the embedding model and measure retrieval quality. Add conversational memory so follow-ups work. Deploy to Hugging Face Spaces.

    Starter code — the retrieval core (chunk → embed → index → retrieve). Predict which two chunks come back for the question before you run it, then add your document loader and the LLM answer step.

    # pip install sentence-transformers faiss-cpu
import numpy as np
import faiss
from sentence_transformers import SentenceTransformer

# 0) YOUR TURN: replace this with text loaded from your own PDFs / notes.
documents = [
    "The mitochondrion is the powerhouse of the cell, producing ATP.",
    "Photosynthesis converts sunlight into chemical energy in plants.",
    "Newton's second law states that force equals mass times acceleration.",
    "The capital of France is Paris, located on the river Seine.",
    "Gradient descent updates parameters in the direction of steepest descent.",
]

# 1) Chunk: here each document is already one chunk. For real docs, split long
#    text into ~200-word overlapping windows so each chunk stands on its own.
chunks = documents

# 2) Embed every chunk into a vector.
embedder = SentenceTransformer("all-MiniLM-L6-v2")
embeddings = embedder.encode(chunks, normalize_embeddings=True)
embeddings = np.asarray(embeddings, dtype="float32")

# 3) Build a FAISS index. With normalized vectors, inner product = cosine sim.
index = faiss.IndexFlatIP(embeddings.shape[1])
index.add(embeddings)

# 4) Retrieve the top-k most relevant chunks for a question.
def retrieve(question, k=2):
    q = embedder.encode([question], normalize_embeddings=True).astype("float32")
    scores, idx = index.search(q, k)
    return [(chunks[i], float(scores[0][j])) for j, i in enumerate(idx[0])]

question = "How do cells make energy?"
for text, score in retrieve(question):
    print(f"{score:.3f}  {text}")

# YOUR TURN:
#  - Build a prompt that pastes these retrieved chunks as context and asks an
#    LLM (Hugging Face pipeline, or an API) to answer USING ONLY that context,
#    and to reply "I don't know" if the answer is not present.
#  - Wrap retrieve(...) + the LLM call in a gradio.ChatInterface.

    For "How do cells make energy?" the retriever should surface the mitochondrion and photosynthesis chunks with the highest similarity — even though the question shares no exact words with either. That's the magic of embeddings: they match on meaning, not keywords. Wire a language model on top to phrase the answer from those chunks and you have a working RAG system over your own knowledge.

    🤖 Boss fight — defend the whole pipeline

    This is the capstone boss. Paste it and don't stop until you can narrate all five stages and explain why RAG beats just asking a bare LLM.

    Quiz me on my RAG Q&A app, one question at a time, escalating. Make me explain each stage in one sentence — chunk, embed, index, retrieve, generate — then go deeper: why embeddings match on meaning not keywords, why I chunk with overlap, what top-k controls and its tradeoff, why I instruct the model to answer ONLY from context, and why RAG reduces hallucination compared to asking a bare LLM. Then give me a failure scenario (a question outside my docs, or chunks too small to hold the answer) and ask what goes wrong and how I'd fix it. Grade each answer, name the gap, escalate. Keep going until I can defend the entire pipeline and its failure modes cold.

    You pass Quest 5 — and the whole track — when you can diagram all five RAG stages from memory and explain why your app says "I don't know" instead of making something up.

    08 · cold recall

    Pull the track out of your own head.

    Re-reading the quest list feels productive and mostly isn't. Answer each card in your head first, then flip. A blank where an answer should be is exactly the signal of what to go rebuild.

    Why must the test set stay untouched until the very end?
    tap to flip
    Any decision made using the test set leaks its information into your model, so the final number stops being an honest estimate of performance on truly unseen data.
    What is data leakage, and where does it sneak in during a bake-off?
    tap to flip
    When information from validation/test data influences training. Classic culprit: fitting a scaler or imputer on the whole dataset before splitting. Fix: put preprocessing inside a Pipeline so it re-fits per fold.
    Why does transfer learning beat from-scratch training on a small image set?
    tap to flip
    The pretrained backbone already learned universal visual features (edges, textures, shapes) from millions of images; you only train a new head to map them to your classes. Too little data to learn those features from zero.
    What does a tokenizer do, and what does the model actually receive?
    tap to flip
    It splits text into subword tokens and maps them to integer IDs, plus an attention mask marking real tokens vs. padding. The model never sees raw text — only those IDs.
    Name the five stages of a RAG pipeline.
    tap to flip
    Chunk (split docs) → Embed (text to vectors) → Index (store in FAISS) → Retrieve (top-k nearest to the question) → Generate (LLM answers using only retrieved context).
    How do embeddings find the right chunk when the question shares no words with it?
    tap to flip
    Embeddings place text with similar meaning near each other in vector space, so similarity search matches on meaning, not exact strings — "make energy" finds "mitochondria produce ATP."
    Lock this in. Quest 5 leans on the embedding intuition from Modules 9 and 13, and every quest reuses the honest-evaluation discipline from Module 6 — the untouched test set returns in your final RAG sanity check, where you test questions that are and aren't in your docs. The thread running through all five: the model's number means nothing until you've measured it fairly.
    🤖 Final boss — the blank-file rebuild

    The real test of this whole track isn't finishing the quests once. It's rebuilding one from nothing. Pick any quest you've completed, then run this.

    I just finished one of my ML quests. Now make me rebuild it from a blank file with no reference. Name the quest, then walk me through it as a strict pair-programming partner: ask me what the first step is, wait for me to write real code, review what I wrote for bugs and leakage and bad evaluation, and only give a hint if I'm truly stuck — never the full answer. At the end, quiz me on three choices I made and push back on any hand-wavy reasoning. Tell me honestly whether I've earned this quest or need another pass.

    You've finished the track when you can rebuild any quest from a blank file, defend every modeling choice without flinching, and explain — pointing at real output — exactly where each model succeeds and fails. That's not a certificate. That's a skill.
